This eminent Specialist, Founder, and Chief

PRIVATE, NERVOUS, AND SKIN
DISEASES.
He has by profound research thoroughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest
and safest methods by which they may be eradi-
cated, with these widely recognised qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to his
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.
YOUNG MEN
who suffer from the results of early follies, trans-
gressions or excesses of more nature ago, bladder
difficulties, a whitish or dark ropy sediment in
the water, accompanied by slight burning or
smarting sensation, Headaches, Drowsiness during
the day, circles round the eyes. Giddiness,
irregularity of the bowels, specks before the eyes,
bashfulness, discontentedness, weakness or pains
in the back, Liver or kidney complaints, dreams,
buzzing noises in the ears and head, pimples, a
fear that something dreadful is about to happen,
weak stomach, yellow or blood-shot eyes, impure
blood, hasty, uncontrollable temper, hacking
cough, who cannot marry, nervousness in society,
wakefulness, palpitation of the heart, general
weakness, and other symptoms which lead to in-
sanity and death, should consult him, for he has
mastered these difficulties. On receipt of 6d. in
stamps he will send secure from observation in a
plain wrapper, postage

SPECIALIST for the Treatment of Nervous
Complaints, Diabetes, Tumors, &c.
Registered by the Medical Boir lof N.S. W. Lite House Sur-
geon to the Great Northern Hospital, Londnt.
I am aware that I am not strictly following the paths
of professional etiquette by advertising myself as a
Specialist for the t; eatment of this class of disease, but,
unfortunately for the suffering public in Australia, the
apathy shown, by Qualified Men in the treatment of
Chronic and Nervous Complaints has induced men of the
lowest grade of intellect (whose only qualifications are
unlimited assurance) to spring up, and by specious
advertisements and false promises trap the unwary.
It is very evident that sufferers from these diseases
have been scandalously treated by these unqualified
persons The evidence taken at the Medical Enquiry
held in Sydney (published in the Daily Telegraph , Nov-
6th 7th and Bth. 1890) plainly shows the disgraceful
manner in which patients are duped. Not only are
they robbed of their money, but what Is of infinitely
greater importance, their health is injured also. It is
therefore time that Legally Qualified .lien entered the
field to fight these pests of society with their own
weapons, viz., Advertising.
It is utterly impossible for those who are suffering
from the>e diseases, who are scattered over oil parts of
Australasia, to ascertain which Qualified Practitioner
makes these diseases his speciality unless he advertises,
I consider therefore, that by advertising I not only
strike a blow at the Quackery which now exists, but I also add
to the usefulness of my profession and in no way lessen my
personal dignity.
My Qualification* I invite the fullest investigation into. For some considerable time I was House Surgeon to the
Great Northern Hospital, London, where I had special opportunities of observing the many phases of the Nervous and
bexua'. System, and to become acquainted with the most scientific methods of effecting their cure.
My sole attention is given to the treatment of these diseases, and when a person consults me he is informed at the
commencement whether X can effect his cure or not. The patient is not deluded with false and specious promises, and
If I undertake the case
I Absolutely Guarantee a Complete and Permanent Cure.
My great success in the treatment of these diseases enables me to give this guarantee without fear.

JUNE 13, 1891.
The Key of Australia.
The Australian colonel, that martial dealer in
cheap shirts and pants who keeps this land in a
constant state of frenzy, is suffering just
now from a new seizure. Within the last
few months a universal desire for forti-
fications has set in, and a sudden demand for
ramparts and entrenchments has arisen all over
this harassed continent. Nearly every seaport
in Australia is now fortified, more or less, and
everywhere the alleged Australian soldier is
fooling with some ponderous iron object with a
hole in the middle of it; but this is apparently
only the beginning of the trouble. The Military
Commission, now sitting in Victoria, has evoked
the surprising fact that Melbourne, despite its
ponderous defences, is practically about as help-
less as it was in the early days when there was
not a single cannon in the whole province,
and when the invader would have had to be
brained with the 'butt-end of an old fowling-
piece. The visitor who wanders in the dark
around the shores of Port Phillip is perpetually
falling down with a whiz and a crash over
£lOO,OOO worth of big guns, but these guns, it
seems, are badly placed; and are not big enough,
and there aren’t enough of them ; and therefore
it is held by the best military authorities that
the harbour should be spotted over with torpe-
does as a further measure of protection. Then,
if the invader ever comes, he will be blown up,
and when he doesn’t come, a P. and O. liner
Avill probably be blown up instead, and by these
extreme measures, combined with some more
forts and a further assortment of big guns, the
“Key of Australia,” it is hoped, may be pre-
served from falling into the hands of the alien.
But while the Victorian Government is dealing
with this problem the authorities in Queens-
land, acting under the advice of another im-
ported officer, are considering the advisability of
erecting a series of ponderous ramparts at
Thursday Island, because that dreary, lone-
some little place is likewise the “ key of Aus-
tralia,” and must be defended at any cost. Also
yet another gang of Imperial officers have
proved conclusively that Albany must be forti-
fied without an instant’s delay, because it is the
only undeniable “key of Australia,” and its
loss would be fatal to the security and happi-
ness of this continent. Some more “key” of
the same description has lately turned up at
Fremantle, which is also in want of cannon and
entrenchments, lestjts loss should burst up this
rising nation ; and another British general has
issued a report within the last month, by which
it appears that Port Darwin is the one genuine
and original “key,” and, therefore, it is
eminently desirable that Port Darwin should be
made impregnable without an instant’s delay.
Further, the Age is of opinion that the Austra-
lians should bear a hand in the defence of India,
because the only authentic “key” of this
country is Herat, and all other keys are only a
base imitation and an infringement of copy-
right.
* # #
In addition to all this, about a dozen places
of more or less importance have been fortified,
to a greater or less in the past, because
each of them was the key of Australia; and
whenever each place was fortified it gave up
being a key there and then, and some other
place had to be fortified immediately afterwards
because it had somehow started to be a key,
and whenever the defences there were com-
pleted the new key of Australia also knocked
oft being a key. Each of these alleged
fortresses furnishes a job for another im-
ported officer and some more inspectors and
colonels, and commanders, and miscellaneous
warriors. Each of them requires some more
standing army to look after it, and each addi-
tion to the standing army furnishes more jobs
for still more colonels. When a new “key of
Australia ” is discovered and fortified, then the
cheap, unimportant warrior in charge hunts up
another key, and makes a job for his brother, or
Ins uncle, or his military grandfather, in looking
after it; and each new key leads to commissions
and percentages on the purchase of guns,
ammunition, torpedoes, and other swindles.
Then the multiplied colonels commence to drift
into Parliament and form a military party
there; and the army is used to hunt down
labourers who are dissatisfied with their wages;
and Australia begins to assume the airs of a
cheap, broken-down Great Power, and to make
an ass of itself generally. And the sum of all
this bellicose drivel is that every place on the
whole Australian coast is the key of Australia
if an enemy gets hold of it, and no place is the
key if the enemy doesn’t get it, while, in the
strictly military sense of the term, there isn’t
any key to Australia or to any other place, and
there never was a key to anywhere. The chief
object of the whole scheme, in short, is to
create a military spirit in Australia, and to
promote that tendency to big guns, reviews,
autumn manoeuvres, and warlike whoopings for
Britain’s gracious monarch, which is the surest
guarantee of the permanence of the empire.
* # *
The projects for the defence of Port Darwin,
Fremantle, and Thursday Island are fair
samples of the garish pig-headedness of the
average colonel. While Port Darwin is left un-
defended no enemy is likely to come there, for
there is practically nothing to get. The place
is the entrance to nowhere, there is scarcely
any population, there is hardly anything to
plunder, and if the town is captured the said
enemy can’t walk overland to Adelaide or Bris-
bane, so that for all practical purposes he might
as well be in the interior of Africa. No invader,
except one fresh from a lunatic asylum, is ever
likely to interfere with such a place for the next
century, for the job wouldn’t pay expenses.
But the imported general has a vague idea,
built up on European precedents, that a sea-
port must be defended simply because it is a
seaport. He knows that in Europe such a place
would be fortified to protect the shipping and
the inhabitants, and he is incapable of realising
that a seaport with no shipping or inhabitants
worth mentioning is in an entirely different
situation. And when Port Darwin is fortified
it is quite probable—should war break out—
that an enemy will come there. He will come
to steal the guns, in the first place, and to
knock out the garrison in the second. If this
country is insane enough to leave half a million
pounds’ worth of guns lying about in an exposed
situation where there is practically no one to de-
fend them, somebody is almost sure to come for
them, and the same thing would happen if a
bank-note was left hanging on a tree. If enough
fortifications are put up to protect the town
then it will require several times the present
population to protect the fortifications, for a
gun can no more defend itself than can the
same weight in old iron. Therefore, it will be
necessary to raise some more standing army to
watch over the guns, and more colonels will be
wanted, and more military demonstrations will
ensue, and some fresh ironclads and torpedoes
will be necessary to “co-operate” with the
fortifications, for, when a new fort is put up, an
ironclad is always wanted to keep the enemy
from getting too near it. Also, there will be
an urgent demand for a “strategic railway,”
because under present circumstances the
garrison would have to run a thousand miles to
reach a place of safety, and reinforcements
would have to walk a thousand miles in order
to come to their assistance. The railway will
be a dead loss, but if an enemy should come it
will be handy for him to travel by. All these
troubles could be obviated by leaving such a
place severely alone, but—in the strategic drivel
of the period—it is a “key,” and must be de-
fended accordingly.
* * #
Unfortunately, the bulk of even the most in-
telligent nation is exceedingly credulous, and
when any Jingo states that the key of Australia
is in danger it generally believes him there and
then, without asking for evidence. It never
stops to reflect on the mouldiness of this ancient
wheeze, or considers the eminent absurdity of the
whole theory. It is impossible for 4,000,000
people to fortify a whole continent, and it is
useless to waste the national wealth in fortify-
ing a few detached spots, for if the defences in
these scattered localities are sufficiently strong
to keep out an enemy, then the enemy—if there
is one—will land somewhere else, and if they
are not strong enough, then the same enemy—if
he exists at all—will capture them, and it will
cost another heap of money to recover them.
The common assumption is that the key of any
country is some mysterious and undefined spot
one or two miles square, and looking exactly
like any other spot, which, for some deep mili-
tary reason, must be defended at all hazards,
and when this place is lost it is all up with
everything and everybody. Yet experience has
knocked this theory into shivers every time the
two have come into contact. Herat is nominally
the key of India, but it has been captured over
and over again, and yet nothing has happened
to India, and Herat has proved a death-trap to
whoever entered it. Calais was the key of
France for centuries, but the English held it for
three hundred years and made no progress, and
now Calais is an undefended town, and nobody
has any use for it. The same rule holds good
all through history, and therefore when
some imported soldier drivels about the neces-
sity for fortifying some remote uninhabited spot
on the ground that it is the key of Australia, it
may be safely assumed that lie is either boom-
ing the great Imperial conspiracy, or else is
looking out for a profitable job. For Australia
hasn’t any key, and it doesn’t matter much
whether it has one or not, and it doesn’t want
one; and when it is inv.tcd to put up ramparts
on some desert spot and then hire a standing
army to look after them, and hoist the British
flag and holler for its beloved Queen, it would
prefer to stand out. And if at any future time
Australia should chance to have a key and some
enemy should capture it, the circumstance
really doesn’t amount to much, for if the enemy
is strong he will do just as much damage to the
country when he has captured the key as if he
had captured some other place instead, and if
he is weak the national forces will knock him
out of the key just as easily as they would
knock him out of any other locality.
The Orange Pot and the Roman Kettle.
The speech delivered by President R. T. Yale
at the annual congress of Victorian Orangemen,
held at Warrnambool, appears in extenso in the
local Standard, and is a fair sample of what
men will say when once inoculated by that most
deadly of all poisons—the virus of religious and
racial prejudice. It is strange that such a
speech should have been delivered at all;
stranger that it should have been spoken when
every library supplies infrangible proofs of its
virulent misstatements ; strangest of all that it
should have been spouted in a land whose
national motto is, or ought to be, “ Down with
your miserable re-incarnation of old-time
Romanist and Protestant feuds. To Tophet
with those who, while the morning twilight of
the twentieth century crimsons the horizon,
show their love for their adopted country by
planting and tending a crop of rank religious
weeds that have done so much in distant
lands to conceal and smother the fair tree of
Humanity.”
* # #
The toast of the evening was “ To the memory
of William, Prince of Orange, who freed us
from the tyranny, of ungodly men.” In pro-
posing it, Mr. Vale is reported to have said ;
They had bitter experience that the Church of Rome
was a church of greed and dogma—a church beyond their
conceptions in its social and political connections. . .
If he had time he would draw their attention more fully
to a fact which could not be denied—that the progress,
material comfort and general happiness of the people was
inconsistent with the full power of the Church of Rome.
For a thousand years the Church of Rome had controlled
the destinies of France—could send her men to the
gallows and her women to the stake. It had one-third
of the real property and one-third of the population. . .
The lords and the clergy claimed privileges and exercised
them, and these were not abrogated until the recent
period of the revolution. The Church did not raise one
finger to destroy these abominable claims of the feudal
laws of France. Coming to the present they should keep
in mind and never forget the essential differences between
members of the Catholic Church and members of the Pro-
testant Church. They, as Protestants, claimed the right
of private judgment, and they considered that no
priest and no bishop had the right to decide one
solitary question on their behalf. . . . Contrast the
position of the people under Catholic domination and
under Protestant freedom—take parts of Ireland and
priest-ridden Spain and the result was unmistake-
able. . . Take the positions in the colony of Scotch-
men and Englishmen—bank-directors, mine-managers,
members of stock exchanges, leading tradesmen, mer-
chants of every kind, and for one Catholic they could
name ten Protestants. Then turn to the names of those
who had joined the great majority, and to the record of
the wealth they had acquired and left behind, and they
would And men with thousands and tens of thousand's
who had belonged to the Protestant churches. He did
not desire to speak with any feeling of bitterness toward
the Catholic Church, and they had no wish to destroy it,
but he simply wished to point out to them these facts.
Protestants did not persecute. [Ours the italics.]
There was talk of abbeys and churches having been
destroyed, but these were not destroyed by men who had
been educated as Protestants, but by those who had been
brought up in the Catholic Church. In Spain, Italy and
other countries, the destruction of churches had not been
caused by Protestants, but by the Catholics themselves.
* # #
The foregoing excerpt does not contain all or
nearly all that the chairman said in proposing
the toast of the Dutchman whose advent freed
England from the “tyranny of ungodly men.”
But it contains enough to show how the simplest
historical lessons may be distorted or read back-
wards when anything is to be gained by doing
so. For the Church of Rome we hold no brief.
Wc do not belong to her communion ; it is im-
probable we ever shall, unless second childhood
should overtake us prematurely; our chief
motive in the matter now under discussion is
the cynical interest we feel in the doings of the
humbugs and fanatics who enslave wage-earning
mankind by making it fight for the shadow
while they themselves eat the meat and gnaw
the bone.
* # #
The world contains no impartial history.
Perhaps the nearest approach to impartiality
was made by the Englishman Hallam. As for
such writers as Hume, Alison, Macaulay,
Froude, and a host of others among our own
countrymen, not to mention the great con-
tinental historians, their productions, although,
in some instances, exquisite as works of art,
are too obviously one-sided to do more than
engender distrust. Still, there are certain
salient facts, the proofs of whose occurrence are,
in one sense, as strong as those of pons asinornm,
or of the impossibility of finding two determi-
nate numbers to represent the diameter and the
circumference of the same circle. In spite of
Archbishop Whately’s ingenious paradox, for
instance, he would be a bold controversialist
who denied that, on a certain June day in 1815,
a French army, commanded by one Napoleon
Buonaparte, was worsted in Belgium. How
that defeat was brought about is quite another
thing—the fact of the disaster is incontestable.
.So with the death of C.esar, the decapitation
of Scotch Mary, the murder of Henry of
Navarre, and the assassination of the present
Czar’s predecessor. The evidence for all these
occurrences is, in its own way, as strong as
for the existence of a large city on the shores of
Port Jackson. Nothing like the same evidence
exists regarding the life and works of Hercules,
of Homer, or of Christ. This brings us to Mr.
Vale’s remarkable statement, that “Protes-
tants did not persecute.”
* * #
We make bold to say that all Christian
Churches—the Quaker and, perhaps, a few
other obscure sects excepted—persecuted when
they had the power, and, in all likelihood,
would do the same again but for being muzzled
by advancing Secularism and widespread scep-
ticism. When Roman Catholicism was in power
it seldom hesitated to draw the sword. It
prompted the slaughter of the Albigenses, of
the Morescos, of the Huguenots. It showed no
mercy to the Lollards in England or to the
heretics in Flanders and in Spain. The abomina-
tions following on the revocation of the Edict
of Nantes were not disagreeable to it, nor were
those which desolated Germany during the
thirty-years’ war. But how did Protestantism
behave when it got in the saddle in
Northern Europe ? The very men who
proclaimed an open Bible and the right
of private judgment were those who muti
hated and massacred their fellow-country-
men who presumed to open the Bible and exer-
cise that right of individual opinion on its
bewildering contents. Of Elizabeth’s reign a
late writer says —“ The gaols swarmed with
recusants. Indeed, it is on the whole hard to
say why Queen Mary should have a monopoly
of the epithet ‘ bloody.’ ” Considering the
savage efforts made by English Protestants
during the ignoble Stuart dynasty to force
Episcopacy on the Scotch Protestants by means
of the bullet and the boot; the sufferings of
English Dissenters who, like Bunyan, failed to
recognise the necessity for bishops, and the long
dark nights of bondage that spread as a pall
over Papist Ireland almost to our own day, it is
difficult to read with calmness the statement in
this our time that “ Protestants did not perse-
cute ” —as if they were not to this hour only less
cruelly persecuting in Wales, because the power
to use axe, sword, thumbscrew, and firebrand
has been wrested from them.
# # *
To find creeds that did not persecute it is
necessary to fall back on some of the old Eastern
beliefs which, unlike Christianity, brought peace,
not the sword, among men. Even religions
derived from Christianity, such as Mahom-
medanism, have adopted persecution as the
shortest road to success. Mr. Yale’s assertion
that abbeys and churches were never destroyed
by men “who had been educated as Protes-
tants ” would be delightful reading in a camp
of Cromwell’s Ironsides or of French Hugue-
nots. The soldiers of the Commonwealth had
even less regard for old churches or stained
windows than the French Republican dragoons
who stabled their horses in Italian shrines and
bestowed moustaches on male and female saints
with a fine disregard of sex. There was some
excuse for the Frenchmen; there was no excuse
for the iconoclasm of the Roundhead saints who,
to use a vulgarism, “travelled” on religion
just as others endeavour to do to-day. All
this goes to show that fanaticism is much the
same whatever guise it wears. Masquerading
in the garb of Catholicism, it prompts men to
starve and scourge themselves in lonely cells, or
to fling women and children into the flames, as
Alva’s butchers did in the Low Countries ;
when labelled “Protestantism,” it burns and
racks and boots its opponent and hates its neigh-
bour for the love of God.
# # #
Mr, Vale accuses the Catholic Church of
greed, of not only permitting but abetting the
horrors of the feudal system in France, and of
raising no finger to abate those horrors till the
Revolution came and made short work of
feudalism and Catholicism alike. Yet this
very Revolution—this magnificent attempt to
rid the land of the monster whose three heads,
Monarchy, Aristocracy and Priestcraft always
belched in accord when heretics had to be burnt
or provinces desolated—this grandest of popular
uprisings in favour of oppressed mankind found
in Protestant England its bitterest and most
unrelenting foe. It was a decree of Napoleon’s
that put an end to the Spanish Inquisition, and,
but for the insensate opposition of mad George
the Third, such wedges of liberalism and secu-
larism would have been driven into Spain and
Italy as would give an uncomfortable squeeze
to the sacerdotalism which was ruining both.
As for greed—a celebrated historian once rose
from his books and proclaimed to the world that
for greed and meanness, he knew of no two
histories to equal those of the Church of
England and of the University of Oxford.
What has that Church through its bishops in
the House of Lords ever done to ameliorate
the condition of the people or lend a hand in
pulling the Car of Progress ? Is it not notori-
ous that the spiritual peers (the very term is
blasphemous) from time immemorial have pas-
sionately opposed all movements whose objects
were to raise or educate the masses? Sydney
Smith, himself a staunch churchman, wrote of
them : —“ It is a melancholy thing to see men
clothed in soft raiment, lodged in a public
palace, endowed with a rich portion of other
men’s industry, using their influence to deepen
the ignorance and inflame the fury of their
fellow-creatures. ”
* * *
If the Roman Church did nothing to abate
the abuses of French feudalism, what has the
Anglican Church done to forward British
liberty ? We are reluctantly obliged to inform
Mr. Vale and his confreres of a few things
it has done in the cause of “Pro-
testant Freedom ” —the words are his own.
In 1807 it was sought to establish rate-'*
supported schools in poor English districts.
The proposal was rejected without a division,
the Archbishop of Canterbury saying: “ Their
Lordships would feel how dangerous it might
be to innovate in such matters. Their Lord-
ships’ prudence would, no doubt, guard against
innovations that might shake the foundations
of our religion.” The faith that might be
shaken if Hodge were taught his ABC cannot
be said to be particularly robust. In 1810,
when an endeavour was made to punish the
larceny of five shillings by transportation for
life instead of death—there were then 223
offences punishable by strangulation the
bishops to a man voted against the proposal as
an innovation “that might shake the founda-
tions of religion.” In .1821, when an effort was
made to allow Roman Catholics to sit in Par-
liament ; in 1822, when it was proposed to let
Catholic peers sit in the Upper House ; in 1829,
when the Emancipation Bill was passed ; in
1831, when the Reform Bill was introduced, the
Anglican successors of the Apostles were venom-
ously hostile. Regarding the last measure,
the Archbishop of Canterbury declared that
it was “mischievous in its tendency, and
would be extremely dangerous to the fabric of
the constitution.” Mainly through episcopal
opposition, it took twenty-five years for the
Jewish Disabilities Repeal Bill to become law,
Archbishop Howley holding that: “ The


moral and intellectual capacity of the Jews
disqualified them for all legislative duties.”
The bishops voted to exclude Protestant Dis-
senters from Protestant universities ; to keep
popular education in the disgraceful condition
ni which it was 50 years ago; to retain religious
tests at universities ; to retain Church rates,
deeming them “ essential to the permanent
welfare of the Church;” to confer on churchmen
alone the privilege of having funeral services
performed over them in churchyards ; and to
disallow marriage with a deceased wife’s sister*.
These are but a few out of many examples of
“Protestant liberty,” as understood by the
princes of the Establishment. To give a man
an open Bible with full liberty of private judg-
ment, and then punish him for exercising the
latter, is not such a great boon after all.
Indeed, if the English hierarchy had had its
way, not only would the British people be now
the most ignorant on earth, but a large portion,
if not the bulk of Mr. Yale’s brother Orange-
men, would find the doors of British universities
slammed in their faces.
* # *
We have said nothing of English divines of
the 17th and 18th centuries—how they licked
the shoes of the pedantic slobberer, James 1.,
and attributed universal knowledge to the
greatest dunce in Christendom—the "dedication
of Mr. Yale’s “ open Bible ” is enough to leave
a bad taste in any honest man’s mouth ; how
they condoned the offences of the lecherous
Charles 11. and preached unquestioning
obedience to his bigot brother James till the
latter attempted to lay a heavy hand on their
ecclesiastical perquisites, when they promptly
turned against the Lord’s anointed; how,
during the regime of the brutal Guelphs, they
crept up backstairs to obtain promotion by
pouring gold into the laps of trulls. These
things are matters of history with which Mr.
Vale is or should be acquainted. At the pre-
sent moment the English bishops draw about
£170,000 a year in salaxhes, &c., besides
possessing the patronage of 2649 benefices valued
at sonxething like £890,000 annually. If Rome
then is greedy, dogmatic, and opposed to edu-
cation, her young English sister is a veritable
chip of the old block. Assuming that, on the
whole, Protestants are less superstitious and
bigoted than Catholics, that is but in accord-
ance with the fitness of things. It is but meet
and natural that a great religious or political
convulsion like the Reformation or the Revolu-
tion should end by leaving both parties, parti-
cularly the party of innovation, less x’eligiously
and politically superstitious than before. So
far as Liberalism is concerned, every Church is,
or should be, axx ad stance upon its predecessor.
Even before our eyes a xxew Protestantism is
being developed which is farther from that of
the Tudors than the latter is from Rome.
Dogmas once thought essential, such as eternal
punishment, and the efficacy of prayer, are
being quietly ignored or ostentatiously denied.
It is far from improbable that our children will
see a Protestantism which will regard Mr.
Yale’s as that of a small, ignorant, and bigoted
sect.
* * *
The Dutch William of the Orange society is
not the monarch of history. The former “ freed
us from the tyranny of ungodly men; ” the
latter gave us continental wars, the national
debt and an oligarchy unequalled for servility
and greed. He was uncultivated, sullen and—
he signed at top and bottom the warrant for the
cowardly massacre of Glencoe. His hungry
followers, like those of George I. subsequently
fell with sharp beaks and talons on the land.
To one of his mistresses he gave 95,000 acres of
his father-in-law’s land which she held till the
Parliament forced her to disgorge. In all Mr.
Vale’s address, however, there is nothing
droller than his charge against Catholics of
dying poor while Protestants die rich. The
very essence of Christianity is not alone to die
“without money and without debts,” but to
live in poverty. In dozens of places in Mr.
Vale’s open Bible he is told in language it
would be impossible to mistake that poverty is
peculiarly grateful to God, and that men should
take no thought for the morrow. To what has
“ militant Christianity ” sunk when its votaries
claim credit for dying rich, the very thing
which, of all othex-s, so stank in the nostrils of
Jesus? To proclaim one’s self a follower of
the ragged Socialist of Galilee and at the same
time rejoice at the prospect of dying rich is
magnificent in the stateliness of its absurdity.
Vale & Co. should read their Bible.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Standing Army.
At the present time, when one hears so
much loud and vociferous nonsense about
the necessity for a strong standing army,
it is worth while for the Australian people
to keep an eye on the course of events in
Chili, where too much army has brought a
prosperous little republic to unspeakable grief.
And having considered the results that have
arisen in that country through too many
colonels and a superfluity of warlike drivel, the
Australians might reasonably consider whether
it isn’t safer after all to run a Commonwealth
with no standing army at all, and trust entirely
to a citizen soldiery for defence against outside
enemies. Apart from its unhappy tendency to
fool around with guns and other destructive
implements, the Chilian republic is about as
well-constructed as any reasonable State could
be. It has a Parliament, both Houses of which
are elected on a much more liberal suffrage
than that of England or New South Wales, or
many other countries which run in democratic
reality behind it. It has a system of Local
Government which is nearly perfect, and a
President who is elected by the male population
on a basis which comes about as near to the
One-Man-Onc-Vote principle as any thing can do
without actually getting there. The qualifica-
tion of the elector is based on wages, and is
fixed so low that any man who works at all—
not being an absolute idiot, or deprived of both
legs —has a vote. When they fixed up their
constitution the Chilians wisely observed that
the snag on which many republics come to grief
is the excessive power of the President, where-
by that gentleman sometimes refuses to
quit at the end of his term, and there-
fore the presidential power is shared by a
Council, nominated by the Legislature, and no
single act of the President is lawful except with
the consent of this body. Education is compul-
sory and absolutely free, and law is so cheap
that almost any Chilian, however poor, can
run a big lawsuit if he wants to. ' Though
Chili is smaller and less fertile by far than
New South Wales, the country has an
immense railway system from which the
Government makes a profit of over £500,000 per
annum; the national debt is only about sixteen
millions, and the surplus has run as high as
£1,000,000 per annum. Barring Switzerland, in
fact, Chili was, for its size and resources,
almost the most prosperous little common-
wealth on earth. But, in addition to its other
advantages, it has a respectable standing army,
and after the great war with Peru, in which
this force covered itself with gore and glory,
it began to put on a considerable amount of
style. It was a splendid force, in its way, and
well-disciplined—which meant that it would
obey any order given by any brutal madman
who was in legitimate authority over it; and it
was full of martial ardour—which meant that it
would brain its own father, when directed to
do so, without the slightest compunction, in
the firm conviction that God would send in the
Government. Also it was enthusiastic,
courageous, and in every other way reliable—in
other words, it was filled with a certain hog-
like callousness and insensibility which comes
of much drill and military exercise, and would
go anywhere and disembowel anybody, or die
in the attempt. When President Balmaceda
went off his elevated chump and broke the
constitution all to shivers, and turned this
splendid machine against the nation and the
Legislature, it was a woeful surprise to Chili.
The army was just what a standing army
ought to be; it got its orders from the Presi-
dent to burn and pillage and destroy, and
because it had got its orders it went straight
ahead. Its members were Chilians, and like
other Chilians were proud of their country ;
but the first essential of an array is unquestion-
ing obedience, and they filled the bill to per-
fection. Any other army, properly trained,
would go the same road, and when the Austra-
lian Commonwealth is floated—if it ever floats
at all— : and the soft-goods colonels have fixed up
a Federal horde of homicides, that force will do
exactly the same thing should the occasion
come. If it doesn’t, then, as an army, it is a
hopeless failure. The one reason Avhy the
gouty, imported generals and commodores
prefer a small permanent force, rather than a
large citizen army, is that the citizen troops
never acquire that servile callousness which
makes them fit instruments for jumping on the
nation—they always feel that they themselves
are the nation, while the regular soldiers’
nation is the army itself, and nothing beyond
it. The country that pays for a big gang of
permanent assassins is simply sustaining a loose
tiger which may eat it up at any moment. It
supports the animal so that it may scare the
tiger next door, but as a rule both of them end
by eating their respective proprietors, or else
they sit on these proprietors, and wipe them
out by slow suffocation. And when one of the
animals fails to do this, it is a sure sign that it
isn’t big enough to be of any use whatever.

A Dirty Trick.
The Victorian Crown Law Department has
decided to let Colonel Templeton personally
meet £4OOO of expenses incurred in the earlier
stages of the Premier Building Association
prosecutions. The official liquidator, believing
himself entitled to act for the P.B.A. in that
affair, ran up a £4OOO bill ere the Government
sulkily took over the work which they could no
longer hold aloof from with safety. In the
meantime, a shareholder in the ruined society
had succeeded in stopping Templeton with a
Supreme Court injunction, and, if the public
had thereupon been content to let the scandal
die a miserable death, it is probable that the
Government would have paid all costs up to
date, and said no more about it. Everybody
knows that the legal ruling by which Mr.
Templeton was prohibited from going on,
except at his own expense, had the effect of
forcing the Government to go on in his place.
Gillies, Deakin, Peaeson and Cos. objected
to the prosecution of their former colleagues
and the Age’s blood-relation, on the ground
that it was “ misguided,” or uncalled-for, and
now Muneo, Shiels and Cos. may point to the
fact that the Age’s blood-relation wasn’t
actually convicted of anything, and say —
“ What did the other Government tell
you all along?” The conspiracy trial aborted,
as a matter of fact. Two of the defendants
were not found guiltless of the bare-faced
swindling alleged against them, but the present
Government refused to institute further pro-
ceedings, and so the long drawn-out agony
lapsed. Even on the main issue, then, the
official liquidator of the P.B.A. showed suffi-
cient cause why the Crown, and not he, should
have initiated these notorious prosecutions.
Furthermore, it must be remembered that a
considerable portion of the costs charged to
Colonel Templeton represents the legal price of
two actual convictions, because the trouble
started with the minor indictments of Mxeams
and Doherty, both of whom are now in gaol.
They were jugged for transgressions of an Act
of Parliament which the Crown is bound, by all
that is financially upright and holy, to maintain
in its integrity. True, the Crown does not
maintain the aforesaid Act. The Crown’s bosom
pals (always supposing that Gillies, Munko,
and such people possess bosoms) are alleged to
have offended against the Act in all directions,
and to be still doing so. The Crown is, at this
very moment, sleeping with one eye open whilst
Mr. J. A. Wallace brings an action against
the “ Premier ” directors to recover a £6OOO
deposit, accepted by them in contravention of
their rules and in defiance of the poor old down-
trodden Act. But the Government’s methodical
neglect to do the right thing is no excuse for
this obvious doing of the wrong thing towards
Colonel Templeton. He prosecuted and ob-
tained the conviction of two men whom the late
Government refused to toiith, after having taken
out warrants for their arrest. Had the Crown
Law department not taken out warrants and
tlien dropped them there might be some shuf-
fling excuse left open to the Attorney General,
who now declines, “after long consideration,”
to pay the expenses incurred by a private
person in the performance of Government duty.
Readers of The Bulletin don’t require to be
told again that the Premier Building Society
scandal was not confined to the cruel wrecking
of that institution, but extended over the whole
history of the trial which resulted from it.
Purves, Q.C., was a prime mover in the prose-
cutions, and although he ultimately became
counsel for the Crown it is quite generally
known that the prosecution was secretly cursing
his zeal all the time. Purves and his juniors
once stated that they would work for nothing
rather than have Colonel Templeton “let in”
by the Crown, and now the Crown is dishing
them out of that £4OOO, or some of it.

A Problem.
The New South Wales Parliament closed up so
suddenly last week that Jersey’s valuable
opening speech was practically wasted and
thrown away. No doubt, however, the poor,
little, washed-out oration can be boiled down
and used over again, and, provided Jersey
leaves out one conspicuously idiotic clause, it
will answer very well. This is the clause in
question :
Angry and evil feelings have been engendered between
men, although they have never had any personal cause of
quarrel, and a cruel weight of widespread misery has
fallen, not upon the leaders, but upon the helpless, inno-
cent members of the workmen’s families. A commission
has been sitting for several months past inquiring into
this difficult and painful subject.
No doubt the families of the workmen did
suffer through the strike, but the constant
inference put forth by the monopolistic
hypocrite that, because they suffered, the
cause was necessarily a bad one, is three-
parts lies and one part slimy idiocy. Can
anyone tell of any cause, good, bad, or indif-
ferent, which did not entail suffering on ‘ ‘ helpless
innocent families?” The early Christian martyr
generally left his wife and children to starve
after his death whereas if he had remained a
respectable pagan he might have lived and kept
them in comfort. Nine-tenths of the individuals
who have died for the glorious British flag have
left their offspring—when they had any—
destitute, and if they had let the glorious
British flag go to blazes this disaster might have
been avoided. Hundreds of thousands of
obscure, unnoticed individuals perished to build
up Britain’s colonial empire, and their deaths
entailed an untold amount of suffering upon
their families which would have been averted if
they had told the British royal race to go out
and throw green apples at itself and build up
its own empire. Hardly anybody ever
sacrificed himself for independence or
liberty or the freedom of religion or any
other noble cause without leaving his family
in the lurch thereby, but no washed-out
driveller rises to curse the famous Six
Hundred because they charged at Balak-
lava without the slightest regard for how
their children would get on after they had
been hashed up by the Russians, or abuses the
men who relieved Lucknow because some of
them got killed leaving their grandmothers
entirely unprovided for. In fact, anybody
may go and get slaughtered in the most
purposeless and idiotic way in the interests
of the most idiotic monarch alive, and he
will be held up to public admiration
even though he left 26 starving infants behind
him ; but if a labourer goes on strike, and his
family grows hungry in consequence, the case
is entirely different. His cause must be a bad
one because his family suffered on its behalf,
and yet almost every other man’s cause is a good
one for exactly the same reason, and.why these
things are no one can tell.
The Standing Railway Committee of Victoria
has bettered the present betterment principle.
It proposes the abolition of Government com-
pensation for land-severance, and will ask
Parliament to sanction the imposition of dif-
ferential rates against the landowners imme-
diately benefited by anew line— i.e., these
persons are to be charged higher rates than
ordinary, so as to make the railway at once
nearly burdenless to the State. The man who
declines to allow a railway to pass through his
land is to have his wishes ignored and must
look to the local authority for any compensa-
tion. If compensation is awarded, the com-
mittee proposes that the local authority should
be empowered to meet it by imposing a special
local rate. If Parliament declines to adopt
these suggestions, the committee is prepared to
resign en bloc. Reforms in railway details are
to follow these reforms in principles. All this
is decidedly cheering. If equal progress were
made in other directions, it would not be long
before it wouldn’t be worth any sane man’s
while to become a large freeholder in Victoria.
Graham Berry’s evaporated boast of “burst-
ing up the big estates ” is coming gradually
within measurable distance of its accomplish-
ment.

Dear Bulletin, — Is it not a singular fact that,
despite the general outcry in N.S. Wales
against dummyism, all attempts to suppress it
have hitherto failed? In no instance of prose-
cution has a conviction been obtained. The
clauses of the’9o Act relating to homestead leasing
encourage rather than decry dummying. The
powers conferred upon the homestead lessee, to
rent or mortgage his country from the very day
of its being gazetted, make the conviction of a
man for dummying almost a legal impossibility.
Until land is thrown open for selection under
more reasonable conditions, the hond-Jidt man
will continue to be crushed out by the monopo-
list, and dummies will increase and multiply.
In my opinion, the acreage should be enlarged,
the rent reduced, and the • conditions modified
before the bond-Jide man can hope to compete
with the rich employer of dummies. On the
station from which this letter is dated, there
are 13 homesteads; one assessed at 2d. per acre,
and the other 12 at 3d. per acre, while the
station only pays about 1 fib per acre. How
can a 10,240-acre man pay the ruinous rent of
3d. per acre, and hold his own despite natural
drawbacks—drought, rabbits, etc. ? The ’9O
Act is said to have been helped through the
House by men who connive at wholesale
dummying. It is a fact that not 10 per cent,
of the homestead lessees, who have obtained
their lands under the ’9O Act, could prove their
hond-fides if called upon to do so. The land is
being selected by hundreds who, so soon as their
five years’ period of compulsory residence has
expired, will quietly transfer their lands to the
holders of their bogus mortgages, from whom
they are now receiving 30s. per week and
rations,—Yours, H.L.
Considering that “ honours were easy ” in
that ponderous religious farce, the trial of the
‘ Lord ” Bishop of Lincoln for illegal ecclesias-
tical practices, there doesn’t seem to lie much
necessity for the congratulations which some of
the Australian Anglicaiis have effusively for-
warded. And it is not easy for a godless out-
sider to see what would have been gained if the
Loi’d Bishop had won, or what, except costs,
would have been lost if he hadn’t. The chief
conundrum submitted to the Bench was as to
whether it was lawful to mix water with the wine,
or whether it was better “neat.” Their Lord-
ships, after quoting many incomprehensible
authorities, which don’t seem to be of any use one
way or the other, came to the conclusion that
mixing the wine and water is like kissing the
choir-girls— no offence unless it occurs while the
congregation are looking. Upon the point
whether it is the ecclesiastical cheese for a
parson to stand with his back to the communi-
cants, the decision is that this is not illegal :
whether it is a sign that the parson afore-
said is ‘‘ no gentleman ” their Lordships
don t trouble to affirm. Another matter
gravely discussed was the habit that Lincoln
had of drinking all the wine that was left over
after the communicants had had theirs. This
practice was declared to be not unlawful, but
it certainly doesn’t resemble what is known
in cultured circles as “square dinkum.” It
was possibly on account of his not doing the
fair thing in this regard that the Bench
refused to give Lincoln any costs whatever.
Ihe case occupied nine days in hearing, and its
expanse of legal drivel of reported argument
and judgment would swamp about 120 columns
of nonpareil. The net result is that both sides
are landed for costs and otherwise are as they
were. But the Church owes them its gratitude,
for if it were not for these occasional ecclesias-
tical prosecutions the people would forget that
there was any State church in England, or that
there was such a thing as ceremonial religion in
the realm. The nation is reminded of the
Church whenever a big scandal or clerical
rough-and-tumble brings Christianity into
prominence.
Melbourne Age advances the old Tory iiyp<
thesis that “ anyone who refuses to honour the
Queen’s name (on her birthday) should remem-
ber that those who have no loyalty to existing
institutions are not likely to prove the most
reliable supporters of whatever may be substi-
tuted for them in the time to come.” This is
equivalent to stating that any Australian—
native or British-born—who protests against
the monarchical system, because he believes it
to be full of hereditary evils, would be always
sighing for a king if he were to shift his quar-
ters to America. That rooster will not fight,
David Syme. The man who hisses a bad show
is ever enthusiastic in praising a good one.
Also, the supposition that those who now gusli
over Her Gracious would prove the most loyal
supporters of a Republic carries with it the
assumption that they are either greasy time-
servers or out-and-out fools. The Age forgets
that it is constantly referring to the fiscal policy
of Victoria as an established institution. Then
why didn’t the majority which had abolished
Freetrade immediately turn round and vote
against Protection ? In short, why has any
order of things been improved upon since the
Flood ?
There is nothing valid in the sentimental
objections urged by Greer, M.P. (N.S.W.),
and men of his kidney, against the employment
of females in liquor-selling. If a woman
so engaged does occasionally hear coarse
language, on the other hand her presence
frequently—in fact, nearly always—restrains
men from loose behaviour and ribald talk.
Morality gains quite as much as it loses by the
employment of barmaids, and it is a gross libel
to assert that a girl is bound to fall if she
follows such an occupation. In extinction of
that slander it is only necessary to point to
many of our worthiest matrons, married to
foremost men, who at one time of their lives
deftly pulled the beer-pump handle and
smiled into the thirsty man’s glass. The
naturally-virtuous girl would be virtuous in far
more trying circumstances than she would ex-
perience in her position as barmaid. She would
still be virtuous as housemaid at a coffee-palace
inhabited by deacons and teetotal politicians.
Creer’s motion for the abolition of barmaids
takes for granted a number of scandalous and
foundationless assertions. Its wording, more-
over, is not restricted to barmaids—it provides
for the abolition of all female labour in hotels,
so that the cook, scullery, kitchen, and
chamber-maids, &c., &c., &c., would have to
vanish before this onslaught of the godly man.
Paragraph from Melbourne Age, printed under
the heading, “Peculiar Case of Theft.” We
quote it in full, because, as it stands, it has a
simple matter-of-course air which we could not
hope to reproduce in a condensed form :
At the City Court yesterday, Tiros. Aitkex, a well-
dressed young man, was charged on remand with having-
stolen a till from the coffee-shop of Mr. John Tilbotson*
on the morning of the 10th May. The police found the
prisoner in the street carrying the till, and suspecting
that ho had taken it from somewhere, took him into
custody on suspicion. They subsequently found the
owner of the till, who deposed to the prisoner having
been in his shop drinking coffee after 12 o’clock on the
night that he was apprehended. For the defence it was
contended that it had not been proved that the prisoner
had come into possession of the article wrongfully," Mr.
Paxton, P.M., said the majority of the Bench considered
the evidence was not sufficient to convict. It was probably
a foolish trick, and they hoped it would be a lesson to the
accused. In some of their midnight walks the vomw
fellows of Melbourne did things which a little more know'-
ledge of the world would make them more cautious over.
In this part of the world young men often obtained more
money than brains. The prisoner was then discharged.
Mr. Panton lias not spoken so prettily since
Lord Beerthirst came before him a long time
ago. He says that “in this part of the"world


young men often obtained (■sir) more money
than brains.” There would be nothing sur-
prising in this if brains had to be carried away
in a till—or rather a skull—by stealth at dead
of night. Otherwise, we fail to see the appli-
cation of the remark. But the majority of the
Bench who “ considered the evidence was not
sufficient to convict,” what genial humourists
they must have been ! If the fact of a young
man being caught in the street at midnight
carrying a till which he had taken away from a
coffee-shop, wasn’t sufficient evidence that the
till didn't belong to him, and that he was—not
to put so fine a point upon a vulgar theft as
Mr. Pa nton and his fellow-magistrates thought
right to do in this case —xfealiiifj it, we would
like to know what they would regard as suffi-
cient evidence ! We fancy they would have
had no doubts about the affair, and there would
have been no mention of foolish tricks, know-
ledge of the world, &c., if the absconder with
the till had been a poorly-dressed young man
without friends. But as the offender was the
son of a lately-deceased and highly respected
Melbourne potentate, lie was discharged with
good advice by these Justice Shallows, who
remembered their own youth and what terrible
fellows they were then.
Don’t let the N.S.W. electors forget for one
moment that if Parkes comes back with a
majority, to a certainty the Government will be
committed to the Draft Federal Constitution.
Parkes, Bruce Smith, and M‘Millan have now
said that the policy of the Government with
respect to Federation is defined by the resolu-
tions of which the Premier gave notice at the
opening of Parliament. Just here we will
quote the essential compilation :—
That this House re-afflnus its opinion in favour of the
federation of the Australian colonies ... it hereby
approves of the scheme for an adequate federal constitu-
tion embodied in the draft bill of (he Convention, but
reserves to itself the right to propose ommissions and
amendments to be fully set forth by the proposer in each
case in a schedule, and to be finally considered if deemed
advisable by another Convention.
Xow this, the Ministers repeat, and we repeat,
is the Government policy. But observe that
the Convention will only be appointed “ if
deemed advisable,” and the amendments and
omissions to be proposed by the Parliament of
the province are to be included in a schedule,
which may, “if deemed advisable,” be con-
sidered by another Convention. There is abso-
lutely no precision of obligation here, nothing
mandatory ; and if Parkes returns with a
majority he will carry his resolutions in spite of
any promise of unlike tenor and in the face of
any belief on the part of the constituencies that
the amendments made in the House are to go
before the next Convention as the sole con-
ditions on which X.S. Wales will join the
Federation. “Me must have a second Con-
vention !” were words never uttered by any
Conventionist till The Bulletin affirmed the
need of holding one. And r could be
more repugnant to the e ■ i.i.ffi Parkes
went into ah < came ’ ban the
holding of a revising, and p< vising,
and re-re-revising Conventions.
Labour Champions.
' v ' UI * LA.ui writes from New Zealand
K.Mviian Knglish paper what he thinks of the
ki-ht nr troubles in Australia, He girds at the
working classes for the “ insensate selfishness ”
with which, “ for the merest trifles, almost with-
out a reason for any dissatisfaction, they throw
themselves out of employment, inflict shocking
privations on their wives and families, utterly
dislocate trade, and throw the whole country
into confusion.” Charles says he has always
championed the cause of the workers, but now
he is utterly ashamed of them, when he sees the
follies of which they are guilty and the gross
and casehardened selfishness which dominates
their dealings. Untaught by experience, “ they
plunge afresh into the excesses which have
wrought their misery and hardened their hearts
against the sufferings of their own flesh and
blood. ” This same pity for the poor families of
the masses is always a strong card with the
spokesmen of the classes—during a strike. We
hear precious little of it at any other time. There
is no inconveniently excessive overflow of bene-
volent sympathy with the suffering wife and
children of the hard-working mechanic when
the labour-market is overcrowded, and “ the
sacred principle of freedom of contract ” is in
full swing, and work is scarce and wages are
dead low. The sufferings of his flesh and
blood come in for a very scant show of
capitalistic commiseration then. But once let
those very sufferings drive him to talk of a
strike, and a wave of godlike pity sweeps over
the land. A chorus of tear-broken voices
beseeches him to pause before taking a step
that will inflict misery on many thousands of
helpless women and babes, and utterly dislocate
trade. And if he doesn’t pause accordingly,
the disgust of the self-styled champions of the
working-classes finds vent in a long discordant
bray.
* # #
Now, how and when and where has the smug
and well-fed .Reverend Charles Clark played
the part of champion of the working-classes ?
What did he champion them about ? Was it
because they had something to complain of ? If
not, what did they want a champion for?
If it was, what did his championship
amount to ? How much was it worth ?
Did it right their wrongs ? Hid it win them
any privilege that was heretofore denied them ?
We could point to a good many noisy cham-
pions of the Labour Party who are always to be
found in the other camp the moment any
trouble arises. So long as the patient working
man puts up with his horse-like lot, he has a
horde of glib sympathisers to praise him for his
exemplary thrift and contentment and industry,
and to discourse finely about the dignity of
labour and the unfortunate inequality of social
conditions and the precious prospect in the
future—always in the dim and misty future—of
a new and lovely regenerate society, in which
all men are to be equal, and the human
rare will bask in the sunshine of universal
brotherhood and peace. We have met these
champions of the working classes before.
Whenever their hearers take them at their
word, and make up their minds to push a step
on the journey towards the promised land, the
doughty knight errants always get in front and
wave their arms wildly, and scream that the
road isn’t made yet, and that there are several
bottomless pits right ahead, and that the only
thing for the Labour army to do is to be good
and virtuous and do their duty in the station
of life to which it has pleased God to call them,
and that the kindly monopolist only wants to be
left alone to make a nice, broad macadamised
highway to the flowery land of the millennium.
The Rev. Charles Clark doesn’t approve of
the present tactics of the working classes.
Will he describe the tactics he does approve ?
Has he anything to suggest except that they
should go on patiently bonding their backs to
the whip as they have done any time the last
few years ? It’s a cheap game to find fault with
proposed methods of reform. You can’t size a
man up by the schemes be doesn’t believe in,
you want to know what he does believe.

Taking Clark’s letter as his text, another
individual who signs himself J. Trowbridge
Critohell writes-to the Pall Mall Gazette
to deliver bis soul of a wail on the gloomy con-
dition of affairs in Australia. “ Heaven only
knows,’ be says, “ what will become of Aus-
tralia, unless the working men there can be
brought to their senses.” Trowbridge is
mistaken. It doesn’t require omniscience
to know .what will happen unless the
working men are brought to their senses.
W e will tell him. The country will continue
to be run in the interests of monopolistic rings
and land-grabbing capitalists. Property votes
will increase and multiply, and manhood suffrage
will go on becoming a more and more trans-
parent mockery every day. Bloated, red-faced,
short-winded men with big gold watch-chains
will still be seen at the club dinner-table ex-
ploding in puffy indignation at every new exhi-
bition of the gross and casehardened selfishness
that dominates the dealings of the pampered
bricklayer or coal-lumper ; the yellow-booted
Treasury clerk will, as the years go by,
develope a more aggressive haw-haw in his
boasting description of the way the dis-
affected lower orders subsided meekly when
he turned out with a badge and - a
baton in the year of grace one thousand
eight hundred and ninety ; and creatures of
the J. Trowbridge Critohell type will speak
of themselves with a patronising air as haying
always been champions of the working, classes,
you know. All these things , will happen in
Australia unless the working men are brought
to their senses. But J. Trowbridge, etc., may
make himself easy. They are not going to
happen. The working men are coming to their
senses—by degrees.
The People’s Opportunity.
Sir Henry Pahkes has rushed the N.S.W.
elections, hoping, by swiftness of strategic manipu-
lation, to take his opponents unawares and return
to Parliament with afresh majority. Under pre-
sent electoral conditions, where Broadacres,
Mortar and Co. have a few dozen votes apiece,
while Thew and Sinew have but one each and are
often disfranchised altogether by reason of being
compelled to wander afield in search of means of
livelihood, a very large amount of success for the
proletariat cannot be reasonably expected. > Yet,
if the Labour organisations hold their forces well
together, and strike a united blow at the polls,
they should make a distinct impression on the
personnel of the next Parliament At present then-
chief danger lies in the self-importance of many
of their leaders, who seem to regard their
own election to Parliament as a matter of the first
necessity. Still, this is the beginning of a new
epoch in N.S. Wales. A large majority of both
parties are pledged up to the chin to support the
introduction of one-man-one-vote, which reform
will be the precursor of the real battle and victory
heralding the Government “of the People, by the
People and for the People. ”

By the end of next-week the elections for 35
constituences will have taken place and 75 mem-
bers will have been elected to the new Parliament.
For these 75 seats there are no less than §48 can-
didates —a fact which is eloquent in its own trans-
parent absurdity. With such a splitting up of
votes, no other result can be possible than a
general representation of minorities. In any such
representation the single voters must go to the
wall while plural voters triumph. It is to be
expected that many of the announced candidates
will be weeded out ere nomination dates, but, as
the first Dominations do not come off until Mon-
day next, it is useless here to indulge in specula-
tive prophecy.
* * #
Of next week's elections the nominations fall in
this order :—On 15th, 21 nominations; 16th, 5 ;
17th, 8 ; 18th 1. Besides these, for elections that
follow after next week, there are—on 17th 1 nomi-
nation ; 18th, 3; 19th, 9 ; 20th, 10. Nominations
later than next week fall—on 22nd, 3; on 24th, 13.

The absurdity of over-candidature is most
keenly defined in such electorates as—Morpeth,
with 13 candidates for 1 seat; Goulburn, 8 for 1
seat; Hartley, 12 for 2 seats ; Paddington. 18 for
4 seats ; Central Cumberland, 17 for 4 seats ; Cam-
den, 12 for 3 seats; Hawkesbury and Carcoar, 8
candidates each for 2 seats : Hunter, Shoalhaven,
Macquarie West, and Wellington, all single elec-
torates, with 4 candidates apiece.
Arthur Barton Paterson, solicitor, one of
the most cultured and talented of young Austra-
lians, will stand for Yass Plains in the democratic
interest. He is identical with no loss a personage
than the horsey-poet “Banjo,” whose “ Old
Pardon, the Son of Reprieve ” and “ Clancy of the
Overflow ” are known to all men. The Bulletin
hopes to have the pleasure of welcoming to the
new Parliament so bright and downright a son of
the soil.

A tremendous house, including twelve
rows of reserved stalls, greeted “The Gondoliers ”
on first night at Sydney Royal, when all the old
favpurities, especially thedivino William, received
warm welcome, the little man being quite
staggered with the warmth of his reception. A
rough passage over from Melbourne and lack of
rehearsal, caused tilings to go slackly, however,
and interest flagged so immistakeably on the first
few nights, at intervals, that it was rumoured that
the management had serious thoughts of getting
the “Old Guard ” or “Marjorie” ready for the
latter half of the season, but business, so
far, has been so big (money going away
every night from all parts of the house)
that rumour has, no doubt, rendered her-
self liable to action for libel. Gilbert-and-
Sullivanish pabulum is apparently an acquired
taste, for at first sight this opera was universally
voted slow and too quiet by those for whom G.
and S. had not catered for some months, but
their fare is toothsome, and the appetite for it
grows with that it feeds on, and “ The Gondoliers”
(now much better handled than on first night) is
gradually playing itself into public favour. The
overture does not strike us as being a very bril-
liant piece of composition, and none of the music
is particularly catchy, the chief favourites up to
date being Guiseppe’s “Royal duties” patter-
song; the matrimonial “Contemplative fashion”
quartette, and the gavotte quintette. Charles
Ryley's graceful dancing, easy style of vocalisa-
tion and shapely limbs placed him witli one bound
on the pinnacle of public favour, but he has
not, so far, as in Melbourne, been bombarded
with bouquets. Elsie Cameron (who, in appearance
and speaking voice, reminds, us of Effie Liston)
does the little she has to do in a fashion that
makes folks wish her opportunity greater.
Florence Young, with a good stage presence and
sweet voice, advertises herself as an amateur
every time she moves foot or hand, but she has
got lots of time, and apparently heaps of ability.
Elton, as big a favourite as ever, has only to open
his. mouth to get a roar, and his dancing is
universally reckoned a feature of the show. As
for the. “ tiny twins,”. Flora Graupner’s voice
seems to want a rest badly, but Violet Varley has
made big strides, histrionically and vocally, since
her last appearance at this house. So much
cannot be said for Syd. Deane, in whom no
marked improvement, in either direction, is
visible. Henry Bracy has not as yet been able to
do very much with Marco, though his “Sparkling
Eyes” song narrowly escapes nightly encore. A
stronger orchestra and chorus have been heard
in this house since the company last appeared
here. Phil. Goatchef’s Piaxzetta scene has some-
what dissappointed folks who had been led to ex-
pect a .scenic revelation from his brush, and
Brunton s Pavilion, though acknowledged to be
clever,'is voted more suitable for pantomme in
critical circles.
** * *
“ The County Fair ” is simply a chunk out
of New England life for whose full appreciation it
is necessary that- the auditor should have been a
student of Judge Haliburton and Miss Wetherell
and be thoroughly conversant with “Sam Slick,”
“ Queechy,” the “ Wide, Wide World,” and later
works of a similar character. It is difficult to
classify Neil. Burgess’s play; in the main it is
village-drama, but here and there an incongruous
farce element is introduced, while the concluding
and only sensation belongs to the region of melo-
drama. Sadie Stringham plays Abigail Prue—
tlie character originally impersonated by the
author in America—in ain angular, high-dried
fashion and rips out her garrulous nasalities
at such a rate that it is difficult to
follow her. She gets most of her laughs by
a liberal display of white flounce and shoe-strings,
and her fall downstairs attired in a bedgown only,
is most artistically hair-raising. Otis, handled
ably by Frank Currier, is a dry, old-fashioned,
reticent bean-pole, whose clothes fit him nowhere,
and who, despite his stage dialect, makes every
syllable clearly understood. The genial Currier is
likely to become a general favourite, and it is a
pity that “ The County Fair” does not afford him
better opportunities and does not give more
legitimate chances for the delineation of the novel
type of character represented by him and Miss
Stringham. Virginia Earl plays Taggs, a tomboy
Bowery-reared sort of creature, in a purely arti-
ficial style, and her chief success is made in a
song and dance in the corn-shucking, barn
scene. Roland Watts Phillips lias one of those
artless maiden parts which she has successfully
handled hundreds of times, and is as easy to her
as eating pie. Arthur Greenaway’s farmer
is bright, easy, picturesque, and free from his
usual tendency to monotone. Frank Harcourt
plays Hammerhead, a chuckle-headed, inferior
sort of villain, ably ; and Frank Ayrton’s jockey
is all right, though smelling more of the New Ciit
than the Bowery. The racecourse-scene on first
night secured a big ovation from a noisy and
demonstrative house packed to its fullest capacity,
but there is heaps of room for improvement, and
some contrivance—a ground-border, for instance—
is badly wanted to preserve the illusion by con-
cealment of the mechanism. “ The County Fair ”
is not, to our mind, much of a play; a man
recovering from brain-fever might turn out one
with a stronger plot, smarter dialogue, and better
curtain tableaux ; nevertheless, judging from the
laughter and applause of first night, it is destined
to have a long and favourable run.

“ The New Magdalen at Sydney Garrick
on Saturday night did not quite “ break the eel’s
knee.” Neither drama nor cast was new to Sydney,
and Oven the weather wept at the joint prospect
and furnished absentees with a conscientious
excuse for keeping away. Far-fetched in senti-
ment, the drama’s emotional ideality was never
vitalised. Janet Aohureh, as Mercy Merrick,
sometimes erupted into spasms of tortured
writhing, and flashed out jets of unsuspected fire,
but these errant beams were about as electrically
artistic as tlie gasps of agutteriiig-outcandlcwick.
Charles Charringtou s Julian Gray was a parsonic
nullity—a nou-bhamelconic study like nothing on
earth' or in the waters above and below the earth.
Mahcit Flemmin"' needs lessons in spooning
baby, if his efforts ufr Horace Holmcroft are
'tli m i.-.t persuasive lie can employ toward d
his best girl ; he never rose to even
.commonplace jealousy, and he kissed Mercy Mer-
rick as if she were his grandmother’s half-sister’s
aunt. Meta Pelham’s Lady Janet Roy furnished
the only dash of vivacity and light-comedy
sprightliness in the general tameness. Emma
Bronton, as Grace Roseberry, was forcible in
clamour and scorn, but her implacable and unfor-
giving hate was never magnificent. Yet a humor-
ous public insisted upon calling the principals
before the. curtain at every opportunity, and the
dramatist’s touches of high-falutin were heartily
applauded. “The New Magdalen ” was to have
been replaced on Wednesday by Pinero’s “ Money
Spinner,'’ introduced to Sydney’s notice by
Wyberc Reeve some years ago, and that comedy-
drama will probably be followed after a brief
season by “ A Doll’s House,” whose performance
at last Saturday’s matinee crammed the bright
little theatre to its capacity, all the stalls being
reserved and the gallery filled at two shillings a
head by undergraduates. The Women’s College
Fund has thereby benefited to the extent of nearly
£2OO. “ Our Flat ” goes up on the 27th.

¥£
Melbourne Princess’s carried a full load
every night last week, nor was a seat allowed to
go begging at the “ Camille ” matinee. Two per-
formances of Dumas’ well-worn drama, four of
“La Tosca” and two of “Fedora” formed a
sufficiently severe test of the “star’s” powers of
attraction, and the phenomenal advance booking
for this current week of “Cleopatra” set the
managerial mind at rest entirely. Evidently the
lamp of Bernhardt will burn brilliantly in'Mel-
bourne until June 27, when the Gaiety company
will substitute leg for the legitimate, mash’em for
passion, gagedy for tragedy, and frisky wench -
ways for frisky French plays. There is so much
genuine delight in the great artiste—so much real
thunder from the amphitheatre, and hoorooing
from the stalls—that the few vacant stares dotted
around the circle are not worth considering. Men
in a certain “spear” must be present at such
functions, and to know that they suffer torment
between the refreshment intervals is some small
comfort for us of lower grade. But we
are irritated when we find that lords of
broad acres and “ shindycates ” contrive to
escape the responsibilities of wealth by linger-
ing behind the intervals, or, in some cases,
neglecting to come back any more. Alike as a
thing of beauty, a producer of sweet sounds, and
a mistress of lovemaking, Madame Sara has, up
to time of writing, won the largest amount of un-
qualified admiration for her Camille, on the first
night. She was more than usually nervous, they
say, and when Madame gets excited she keeps the
house tingling under the shocks transmitted by
that big battery of nerves which constitutes her
whole being. She is a pictorial triumph directly
she appears upon the stage with her arms ex-
tended like angels’ wings, or divine danger-
signals, expressing her gratitude for the applause
she seems to deprecate. Then she is such a deli-
cate, fading-away sort of Camille, and her voice
through each stage of the pulmonary affection
reminds one of the bells that the sexton sets ring-
ing for Sara when the evening sun is low. This
is' not vulgar and irreverent, we hope. If our
father’s son is low likewise it’s because his heart is
in the right place. When a subject is very
serious the world yearns for comic relief, there-
fore these ghastly jokes are allowed to drop
with a sickening thud into the middle of
the notice. Bernhardt varies much her aspect
and manner of speech with each character, seem-
ingly, so that her admirers might feast on
forty plays for forty nights without finding the
banquet monotonous. As Fedora, she is a nice-
looking, but not very juvenile princess, whilst her
La Tosca has an air of being young and winsome,
rather than lovely and alluring. True to the text,
she represents an operatic divaoi mountain growth,
who holds sassiety cheap—an amorous girl trans-
formed by circumstances into a tigress. After
becoming a tigress she reverts back to her original
state, during the short prison-scene with Cavara-
dossi. In her anxiety for her lover she seems to
forget that she has just slunk from a chamber of
horrors, where, in the mingled lights of wax-
candles and cold grey dawn, the corpse of Scarpia
lies with a crucifix upon its gory bosoni. Which
is perfectly natural, no doubt—anyway, the in-
fallibility of Madame Bernhardt as an actress is
good enough to abide by. Her “ make-up ” and
“ get-up ” are certainly open to occasional critic-
ism, however. If women admire all her costumes
in “La Tosca,” the men do not. They may be
high art, like Holman Hunt’s pictures, but one or
two of them are incomprehensibly ugly. Finally,
Madame is erratic in her treatment of her face for
this part. Sometimes the pigments are laid on with
a careless hand, as though she had been pushed for
time, and on these occasions the effect is more
patchy than pleasing. Metis, asscz lien, as we used
to say in l aris when we went on a Cook’s excur-
sion. The prevailing characteristic of the com-
pany is quiet— i.c., quiet as the race go—natural-
ness. They are not continental barn-stormers by
any means, and Munie, the chief comedian, doesn’t
invariably tell his funny stories to the orchestra.
The men, excepting Fleury, the jeun 'premier, are
apt to look old for their characters, and most of
them, Fleury very much included, are common-
place in modern clothes. But they are picturesque
enough in “La Tosca,” and will cut satisfactory
figures in the ancient historical productions.
Duquesne (the Scarpia) is a strong leading man,
and of several more or less young ladies Mme.
Jane Mea is the one for whom a fellow would
prefer to die if death were an unalterable clause
in the contract. This, as goes without saying, is
exclusive of Madame Bernhardt. They keep on
dying for her all the time, figuratively speaking.
It’s only fair. She herself dies to slow music in
every character.
* -* *
“ Cleopatra ” has disappointed many high
expectations. Bernhardt is, in this instance, a
beautiful and costly show, and fine French taste
distinguishes many of the appointments, but it is
certain that the public looked for more solid mag-
nificence. Only the third scene, which is poetical
without being .expensive,;gained applause when
tile curtain rose on it.' Sardou’s work has
striking situations and occasionally rapid action,

yet it does not display Sardou’s abilities to the
full. The actress harps too often on one string,
and she herself says that Cleopatra is not a very
great part for her. On Monday night, for
the first time, she failed to keep the house
always under her spell, and the gods very
properly protested against those weary intervals
for which Sara is so famous, and during which
she ib supposed to be writing her memoirs on the
sliding roof of the Princess’. Dreadful cries of
“ Roll up, Sairey !” were heard, and the boys also
explained that Britons never shall be slaves, while
a spirit of unrest manifested itself once or twice
in the dreamy third act, when the low comedian
came on disguised as the heathen who brings news
of Antony’s marriage. His comic tones upset
some people’s centre of gravity, and the serpent
of old Nile looked angry, and exclaimed
“0, enough ! ” in the purest English.
Rumour had it that Sara afterwards grew
scornful in her dressing-room, saying that
folks who objected to long waits need not wait at
all, and some of them did depart without getting
their full money’s worth—they would not lose
their last tram even on Sara’s account. Never-
theless, the great artiste must have listened to
reason, for the two finishing acts were rushed
through in time to send everybody away by 11.30.
Cleopatra died of her live-asp bite in about 20
seconds, and the ending of this historical occasion
was as tame as the little snake. The
Roman portion of the company shone to
great advantage, looking their characters
in every case, and a surprise was given
by the Antony, who was impersonated by
a haughty, handsome personage named Darmont;
his acting is neither subtle nor powerful, but his
aspect accounts for his privileged position in this
particular bill. Every reserved seat is booked for
the week, and “Cleopatra ” will be put on at the
Saturday matinee.
