“ Poor Jonathan,” an American version of
a Herman comic opera by tire “Beggar Student’s ”
composer, broke out at Melbourne Opera House last
Thursday. The inauguration of this novelty was
an apparently aimless enigma, but by Saturday a
real sense of humour had begun to struggle through
the gloom, and the reason of Jonathan’s success
in New York stole gently o’er the understanding.
Vanderbilt (a person much esteemed by the Yanks
on account of his inherited dollars) is here skitted
under the thin disguise of Yandergold, described
on the programme as a “ pessimistic millionaire.”
Jonathan is head cook to this morbid monument
of unearned increment, and gets cast from the
kitchen because he tries to fabricate toothsome
confections with a bar of yellow soap and make
delicious side-dishes out of night-lights. Vander-
gold, being biliously enamoured of a maiden who
holds off a little, decides to swap fortunes with his
discharged hash-brewer, and makes over his
wealth to that low-born idiot on the understanding
that they shall both die like dogs directly
either of them ehoses to sing a certain dreamy
melody. In the next act, whilst Jonathan is
wallowing in wealth at Monte Carlo, his bene-
factor turns up in a two-guinea suit and
endeavours to warble the song of destruction, but
his “ear” is at fault, and after wandering about
into several wrong keys he retreats, whereupon
Jonathan comes out again from under the table
and everybody goes on living as before. Finally
Jonathan and Yandergold resume their original
places. The millionaire, no longer pessimistic,
marries his merry maiden (she in the meantime
has figured as prima-donna to a gaudy im-
pressario), and Jonathan returns to the bar-of-
soap business with his wife, Molly, an exalted
scullery lady, who certainly made the “pieces”
fly during her interlude of grandeur. The idea
of the opera is distinctly Gilbertian, but the
librettist (or adaptor) has fallen a mile short of
that idea. At first sight, too, the spectacle
strikes cold to the male chiest in consequence of the
costumes —allowing for theatrical license—being
built on every-day lines, and nothing in the shape
of legs or ballet getting a show. But this objection
wears off considerably after a second visit. In
fact, “Poor Jonathan,” pulled together and
written up, might grow on the public
taste, if given a fair chance to take
root. The airs are tuneful and catchy
in many places, and the whole score is
respectable from a musician’s point of view. Also,
the company are settling down comfortably into
thsir parts, with the exception of Woodfield, who
represents the dyspeptic Yandergold, and has
little to do beyond trying to settle himself. Miss
Clara Merivale negotiates .all but the top notes in
good style, and her comedy, especially the eye-
play, is arch and pretty. Miss Ida Osborne comes
out well everyway as the promoted Molly, whilst
Leumane, after a tiring attempt to be funny in
his cook capacity, becomes a humorous Jonathan
later on. Moreover, his bit of mock-tragedy sug-
gests that Providence possibly intended him to
shine as Macbeth. There’s no telling. Snazelle,
with a new, bright variety of topical song, is the
beau ideal of an impressario, and the rest do
nothing in particular indifferently badly. Money
has been spent on the mounting, and spectators
who can overcome an impression that the supers,
came from the “first part” of a variety show,
will find some points in “ Poor Jonathan ” if they
search for the same.

At the Bijou they were to withdraw “The
Idler” on Tuesday, to make room for “The
Profligate.” The Prof, was an Idler in his time,
Satan found mischief for him to do, and his sin
rises up against him when he wishes to be respect-
able. The play is accepted as Pinero’s finest
effort thus far in his literary career.

The Royal and Alexandra manage to hold
their own against the Bernhardt boom. “Bridget
O’Brien” has another clear fortnight to rage away
at the former house before the Firm’s lease falls
out, and “ The Scout ” will last over Saturday at
the Alexandra, unless they start a new Wild
West drama, with even thrillinger effects, on that
date.

The return of the Halles to Melbourne
Town Hall lias not excited the populace in any
marked degree, and the enthusiasts who couldn’t
satisfactorily account for their emotion last time
seem now to have forgotten that life wasn’t worth
living unless “ the Knight and his lady ” were
Interpreting somebody’s loftiest conceptions.
Fashion is no longer a factor in the attendances,
and only the people who enjoy sonatas and so
forth are scattered amidst a large expanse of deso-
lation —the dead-heads invariably being well to
the fore. Lady Hall 6 and her husband make
glorious music when they choose to minister to
the multitude instead of to a cult—a cult that
never by any chance pay for their seats ; and
Mdlle. Fillunger, the vocalist, is a favourite.

Mr. Jude, a well-known song-writer and
“musical evening” entertainer, whoso name
advertises itself, was to open a season at the
Athenaeum on Monday last, and is sure to be
appreciated. He will revive memories of John
Parry and Henry Russell on five nights of the
week, but the Lynch Family Bellringers, long-
absent from Melbourne, have secured the
Athenaeum for a term of Saturdays. They started
ringing last Saturday, and had a great reception,

At the Waxworks there is a promising
novelty in an engaging young creature who sings
prettily, ties ropes round people, photographs a
list of names on her memory, and makes herself
agreeable all round—a bewitching monologuist, in
fact. Things are getting very toney at the Wax-
works nowadays, and a new sacred group, “The
Widow’s Mite,” is much more of a work of art
than one expects to meet in any gathering of
effigies. The music-halls are not much affected by
French play competition, and othe'r matters main-
tain their equilibrium.

Sara Bernhardt’s engagement is based on
the assumption that she can, within a given
period, draw a larger proportion of the population
in Australia, than in any other country. A fort-
night has been the limit of her American seasons
in cities considerably more peopled than Mel-
bourne. As for England—well, we never heard of
Sara performing out of London (like the Christy
Minstrels), and her visits there are as short and
sweet as the angels’. The knowledge that she has
been, practically speaking, backed to beat her
record may inspire the actress to do ditto in an
artistic sense. Williamson, who has watched
Sara acting scores of times, said that he never
saw her so great as on the opening night at the
Princess’s.

Tom Thorne was recently presented with a testimonial to
commemorate his “ continuance and successful manage-
ment” of the Vaudeville Theatre for 21 years. Thorne
had two senior partners at the outset, Harry Montague
and David James. Montague, the women’s idol, erstwhile
George Kignold’s rival as a beauty (the New York women
were divided into two camps over the pair), died in a
New York garret.
Sara’s garments are built round her, like Sardou’s
plays.
Jeanne Bernhardt, Sara's sister, used to travel with the
star. She had no talent, but shone in a reflected light,
like Rachel’s three sisters, a/.d her brother, all per-
formers.
Jamraoh, the menagerie-importer, used to have great
strength. His Royal Bengal Tiger escaped, right into
Ratcliffe Highway, and knocked down a boy with his
paw. Jamraoh jumped on the tiger’s back, choked him
with his thumbs, while an attendant smacked him over
the forehead with a crowbar - then sold him for £3OO.
Playing at London Strand many years ago, Jennie Lee
lost half-a-crown bearing the inscription “Jo,” which she
had kept hitherto as a mascotte. The coin turned up the
other day in Auckland, and it once more jingles in her
pocket. Some other versions of this yarn are in circula-
tion, but this is just as true as any of them.
The two Duvals, father and son, in “ Camille,” were, in
one sense, never so well depicted as by the Holloways,
with their wonderful resemblance. They reminded one of
the Brothers Webb, wdio used to make up for the
Dromios as perfectly as the Holloways did for the Anti-
pholi.
The Bernhardt books of the play are all flam, and the
press is much to blame for cracking them up. They don’t
give the text, only what is called the argument—a lot of
bosh. Y r et the covers say, “Only version authorised
by Mdme Bernhardt.”
The insuperable drawback to the Melbourne Royal
these times is the vestibule, lined with bars, past which
all the patrons have to go, save those of the dress-circle.
Coppin seriously considers gutting the whole scandal cut,
assimilating the house to the Opera House, Bijou, Prin-
cess, and Alexandra, where such things are banished —
shadows of the old era when the “ Vag-.” slaughtered the
Saddling Paddock. Here is the sole sinful reason why the
Royal has sunk to be the fifth Melbourne playhouse.
W, and G. are sure that Bernhardt’s hit will fetch over
Irving.
You will see the journalist in the gallery, and the can-
vasser in the dress-circle.

Bernhardt has acted three male parts with distinction.
The first is Pierrot, the second Zannetto, the minstrel
boy, in Coppee’s “Le Passant,” and the third one of the
young princes in Casimir Delavigne’s “Enfants d’
Edouard.”

Fillis’s Circus is expected shortly in Australia, from
India. This show, organised in South Africa, will vie
with the best of Chiarini, Wilson, Harmston, and so on.
Thespian belief in the horse-shoe is universal, and even
theatres are designed horse-shoe form, for luck. Mrs.
Bancroft stuck up a gilt horse-shoe 2 feet long in her
attiring-room, London Haymarket Theatre, and they
figure in all playhouses.
Toole acted in three theatres one night—Surrevo
Adelphi, Sadler’s Wells. Had to change hastily in the
cab, from the clockmaker’s boy, in “ Janet Pride,” to
old Master Grinnidge, in “Green Bushes,” but whe’h he
got out the jarvey was frightened, wanting to know what
he’d done with that ’ere boy.
A week or two ago we hazarded a prediction that the
failure of the new London Olympic would drive Wilson
Barrett to Australia, and now it is definitely announced
he is coming. The fact is he got rated too high, for “The
Lights o’ London” and “Silver King” fixed his limita-
tion.
Sara has occupied the sumptuous, bronzy-papered
dressing-room fitted up for Nellie Stewart, and is in
raptures. As the flowers don’t bloom in the spring tra-la,
just now, they couldn’t furnish her with her favourite
big bowls of white roses, and the clinging stephanotis.
But she cries “ Vive Melbou-r-ne !”
Dr. Neild: “She is indeed divine.” James Smith :
“ What aje ne sais quoi!” Frank Stuart: “ What a—
oh!” Councillor Bill levers: “Just the same as in
Parry.” Williamson; “By jing, what a child she is!”
Fitzgibbon: “Sara, toi qui j’aime !” Garner: “Forgive
me, Jimmy, over Johnny Toole.” James MacM.: “Bet
you there’s more money in ‘ The County Fair.’”
When Jenny Lind first acted Alice in “Robert Le
Diable” there was terrific excitement as she clung to the
big stone cross, and Bertramo, the Mephistopheles, tried
to drag her away. A friend asked, “ Oh, what were you
thinking of at that time?” “Well,” said she, “I was
thinking how’ I’d trim my old bonnet.”

fascinations of the unholy woman who is just now drawing
so many Melbournians within her meshes.”

O’Callaghan’s Apple.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ The matter vomited by Private O’Callaghan, of the
Gympie Mounted Infantry, who was said to have been
poisoned by strychnine placed in an apple given bun by
a unionist at Barcaldine, was forwarded to the Govern-
ment Analyst for examination. His report, which has
been forwarded to the Commissioner of Police, stales that
the matter contained no trace of strychnine or any poison
which could produce the effects described.” — Daily
Paper.
O tame result! O’Callaghan,
For weeks we have been thrilled
With horror of the wretch who gave
To you an apple filled
With deadly 7 poison. Since the Strike
Deluded primal man
No apple-yarn made such a stir
As yours, O’Callaghan.
No incident throughout the striio ,
Bloody, and mad, and tierce
As many were, so wrung our heart,
So melted us in tears.
We heard with grief the deeds of men
Splashed with that evil chrism
The shuddering daily papers name
As the “New Unionism.”
We heard that labourers who called
Each other Bob or Jim
Seeing an Officer would cry
Profanely “ Joe !’’ to him :
We knew that troops had been received
With storms of oaths and howls
While in their hearing —insult dire—
Someone had mentioned “ fowls.”
We learned from various sources that
Machiavellian plans
Were being hatched, and dynamite
Concealed in billy cans ;
We read from documents secured,
After a struggle tough,
The writers wished to be supplied
With “ more of the same stuff.”
We heard about a scoundrel who,
Yet roaming earth unhung,
When others cheered our Gracious Queen
Put out his ribald tongue ;
And that damned miscreant who scared
The North out of its wits
By saying that the shearers yet
Would give the squatters “fits.”
These things were horrible, and we
Groaned audibly to read
Of each, but your affair was of
A more demoniac breed ;
They troubled us beyond belief
Yet nothing that we felt
Approached our anguish at your tale,
O miserable Celt !
One afternoon, as you strode dov/ii
The street, a joy to see,
In the full regimentals of
The Gym pie Infantry,
Some murderer a pippin on
H is coward hand outstretched,
You grabbed it and did eat thereof,
And then, poor wretch, you retched.
You took it unsuspectingly
And placed it in the chasm
That intersects your features, and
Chewed on till many a spasm
Shook your great frame —we here assume
That you are strongly built—
Then down you dropped, and up you curled,
And out you yelled—“l’m kilt!”
They gathered round you, pale, aghast
And trembling, one and all,
And everyone declared the deed
Was diabolical;
“Our special correspondents ” wired
To that effect—like birds
That hunt for worms, they saw a chance
Of several hundred words !
And some who felt the honour of
Old Ireland was at stake
Made preparations so that you
Might have a roaring wake ;
While others brought two doctors, who
At once hauled off their coats
And emptied you with stomach-pumps.
Filled you with antidotes.
With scientific skill they sought
To baffle Union hate,
And searched you through for traces of
Corrosive sublimate;
They tried for prussic-acid ; then,
Believing that you might
Have, after all, been only dosed
With modest aconite,
Prescribed for that, and, to make sure.
Full many a sped -fie
They gave you against cyanides,
N ightshade, and arsenic,
And, fearing that the venom might
Not have been one of these,
They did their best to shore you up
Against cantharides.
They thought of digitalis, and
For Indian hemp looked out,
“Precipitates” they grappled with.
And quickly put to rout.
In puzzling over what had brought
You near to Kingdom Come
There even flashed across their minds
The thought of Queensland rum.
But, finding that the Unionists
Were not, with all their faults.
So fiendish as to give you that,
They probed for fatal salts.
Carbolic acid never once
They let get out of view,
They meditated seriously
Concerning Prussian blue.
For spirits of salts they found a balm.
And, with perplexed brows,
Tried on a little thing of theirs
for “ Don’t-Die-In-The House
For morphia one might fairly say
That they were “on the job,”
While strychnine at the very start
Received one on the nob.
To show the murderer that ho
, Had not to deal with flats,
They made you safe and whole against
Tlu- fiercest “Rough On Rats;”
They slew all agents strong to slay
Or elephants or bugs,
And pulled you through, O’Callagh.n,
With stomach-pumps and drugs.
our comrades vowed to have revuige
I’or all you had gone through,
Upon the hell-hound who had wrought
_ His base design on you,
For (wery drachm of nauseous stuff
You topic that day, they swore
To have a fair Imperial pint
Of his false heart’s black gore.
1 lie main camp was surrounded by
Their most unfearing braves,
A ho straightway haled before your Led
Some guilty-looking slaves.
But all iu vain; the miscreant
You said, was not of these—
He had escaped to perpetrate
Yet further villanies !
Ah ! well, the press might warn the world
About the dread abysm
Society is being led
To now by Unionism ;
And well might law-abiding men
At every failure quake;
And mutter that our liberties
Are once again at stake !
We trembled then, O’Callaghan,
But nourished still the hope
A SUFFICIENCY.
TROOPER: “Come along!”
STRIKER: “What for?”
TROOPER: “I heard you hollerin’ out; ‘We don’t want no more Queen I'”
STRIKER: “Well we don’t, ’cause we got more’n enough already.”
That your most fell assailant would
Be surely brought to rope ;
We knew the long arms of the law
Would seek him far and wide
When once ’twas known what he had sought
To place in your inside.
We waited hungrily to hear
What devil’s mixture had
Been hidden in the pippin which
Made your poor iu’ards bad,
And now we know precisely what
Caused you to squirm and twist—
You’ve luoled yourself and us, but not*
The Public Analyst.
He says the apple was all right, ,
And, speaking as a judge,
Dedans your fearful poison yarn,
O’Callaghan, was fudge.
You’ve soared to fame by a mistake,
And made us spoil our “ wipes ”
With grief for—what? Alack, but this—
A Private with the “ gripes !”
Sydney.
C.R.
W. G. Spence, President of the A.S.U. '

A Melbourne child’s epitaph :
NELLIE MUNROf
Her little frame lies chilly
Beneath the green sod,
Her soul is a white lily
By the white throne of God.
Sometimes you can’t beat cross-examina-
tion for eliciting facts. When they were enquiring
in Melbourne the other day about the loss of the
Craigburn, a witness was asked —“ Where was the
vessel when she struck ?” and the answer came
short, sharp and decisive —“ On the rocks.”

Miss Evelyn, going against the venerable
Ilurlbert, alias Wilfred feurray, for breach of
promise in London, read out of her diary in the
witness-box. Here is the purple patch about their
first ‘ Rode in ’bus to Sloane-street.
After shopping, got into ’bus going to West
Kensington, in which I saw a gentleman. An
inexplicable feeling seized me. It was as though
an electric shock passed between us, and as I
could see he was experiencing the same sensation
as myself, I was greatly agitated. He never took
his eyes off me. When 1 got out, Faust followed
me.” This Romeo is 64, and stout. Bearing in
mind, too, how sexagenarian Duncan mashed
M iss Knowles, it must be allowed that the gay old
birds are having their innings, becoming rejuve-
nated Fausts, without knowing it. When, oh
when, will Mrs. Grundy be turned into Juliet ?
It is not very likely, we think, that phono'
, . J , •Q, .i V i 1 ,
graphic w,Usreproduced from the cylinder, will be
valid in English law. In the first place nobody can
swear to the identity of a phonograph voice eon-
seqnentiy the wicked nephew, who was really cut
off with a shilling, might turn up after the funeral
with a mysterious ey inder bequeathing him every-
thing. He could talk uncle’s voice down the tube
for himself and get a crooked solicitor o s vear k
neard the old gentleman do it. Secondly the
disappointed relatives might oppose probate, on
the ground that any man who has a will drawn up
by a lawyer in order that he might speak it into
the telephone instead of signing the document, is
of unsound mind. Or that a man who delivers his
last testament extempore must be madder still.
No, the phonographic will can be never more than
a beautiful dream, legally speaking, and all idea
of forming a family circle round a sort of sewing-
machine, which will proceed to grind out legacies
and add codicils relating to the residue, must be
unwillingly abandoned. We shall never get be-
yond the dried-up old geeser in spectacles who
drones forth the bequests of his deceased client,
and bows to each legatee as his or her name is
mentioned. He is a rasping kind of institution, is
the will-reading lawyer, but there’s human nature
in him after all.' He regulates his bows according
to the legacies and gets almost emotional over the
big amounts, like a secretary reading the list of
subscriptions at a Benevolent Society’s dinner.
Mrs. E. S. Phelps writes: “There is an
indescribable something in a modest girl’s eye,
distinguishing her from the matron.”
The non-legal public is refreshingly tickled
by hearing “ that one of the reasons why the
N.S.W. Law Institute prosecutes Solicitor Henry
Scott Harden, is because it fears that Harden will
‘ pile up the costs’ against those whom the Trade
Protection Society, which he represents and which
is alleged to consist chiefly of himself, prosecutes.”
Also, because “the whole proceedings are calcu-
lated to bederogatory to the profession.” Which,
being interpreted non-legally, means that the
Institute is wroth with Mr. Harden for doing
legal work cheaply : for, since his costs against the
potted defendants would always be taxed by the
Courts, there can be, little or nothing in the “piling-
up” allegation. Though the Law Institute has
been “ moving in the matter for several years,” it
declares that it does “not desire to punish,” but
simply “have it definitely settled whether such
class of business is permissible.” The Protho-
notary is to enquire “whether Harden is solicitor
to the society, or whether he is simply solicitor to
himself.” The public doesn’t care a dump which
he is, as it wants to sec the monstrously privileged
legal ring burst up.

to 3s. Gd. per day, working ten hours a day. This
is “freedom of contract.”

An old-time Sydney police-court solicitor
was “Jim” , to secure whose professional
services was the delight of the “talent” when
“in trouble.” But Jim had a little trouble him-
self once. Something about fees or clients-money,
or one of those things that cron up often as “In
re a Gent, one,” &c., &c. The Supreme Court
called up “Jim” to explain, and Jim’s explana-
tion was at once novel and successful. He had
just lost Ins wife and brought the whole of his
children, seven or eight in number, dressed in
sombre black, each with a white handkerchief
carefully displayed and ready for use, to the
Supreme Court. Alfred Stephen, Hargraves, and
Peter Faucett were on the Bench. Jim’s appeal
was pathetic. He hoped their Honors would take
into consideration his motherless babes and give
him another chance. This was the cue for the
kids, who set up an unearthly yell and flourished
the handkerchiefs. Sir Alfred sternly gave his
judgment, “To be struck off.” Hargraves, in-
clined towards mercy, went for “another chance,”
while kind old Peter Faucett’s heart went out to
the motherless children—he voted with Hargraves
and Jim was saved. Coming out old Bill Roberts
said : “Well. Jim, I never thought you could turn
on the tap so well.” “Go to ,” said Jim.
“ Had you been in my place”—but we won’t finish
the sentence as James did.
The Marquis of Lome and
the Princess Louise are, it is
repeated, “ willing to come to
Australia for the Governor-
Generalship.” The Bulletin
cordially wishes them and all
the other members of the Royal
family health, wealth and pros-
perity so long as they stay away
from here, and it hopes that
they will grow fat and increase
and multiply until, magnifying
to the proportions of a back-
block rabbit - pestilence, the
people of England offer
each for their scalps. But
we trust that they will all
be struck by lightning ere a
single member of the Mon-
archical brood is permitted to
infest Australia permanently.
Parkes boasts that he is
going to the constituencies on
the Eederation question and on
the Convention Bill. This is
more than he can do. Notice
a few “test” questions which
various political leagues intend
making a sine qua non each for
its own members’ votes ; Free-
trade Resurrection! sts, Free-
trade absolutely ; Protection
and Political Reform League,
protection; Good Templars,
prohibition without compensa-
tion ; Single-taxers, their own
sweet fad ; Labour Leagues,
labour reform ; Soeialists, State
nationalisation; All-sorts, one-
man-one-vote ; and with almost
everybody in favor of some sort
of Australian Federation, but
dead against the Convention
Bill as it stands. The more the
mixed-up situation is considered
the more clearly it becomes ap-
parent that the only reliable
method of ascertaining the
opinion of the people on Federa-
tion is by the referendum. So-
called representative govern-
ment is about the most un-
representative system under the
sun.
Lord Hartington was to have gone through
the ceremony of marriage with the widow of our
old friend Manchester sometime in Apri l . The
joyful event hasn’t been announced yet, however
Rev. Howard Macqueary, a priest of the
Protestant Episcopal Church of America (in com-
munion with the Anglican Church) lately wrote a
treatise denying the immaculate conception and
the resurrection. His Bishop, Dr. Leonard, of
Cleveland, appointed a Court of five eminent
ecclesiastics to try Macqueary as a heretic, and
that Court has just found him guilty—by a
majority of one! So that two of the foremost
Episcopalian authorities in America have judi-
cially ru'ed that the denial of Christ’s virgin-birth
and resurrection are not inconsistent with C. of E.
doctrine ! Dogmas are cracking visibly on all
sides.
The latest American perjury is about a
gentleman in Chicago whose skin falls off him
every year. About July his last year’s hide
begins to wither and break up, and presently it
comes away entirely, and for a week he has to sit
around in his flesh and bones, without any clothes
on, and with his vitals considerably exposed.
Then a new outside grows on him by slow instal-
ments, and he is all right for another 12 months.
This is regarded as very remarkable by the local
departers from the truth, but if they saw how
Parkes’ programme shrivels up every year, and
saw him shedding withered Bills and Acts of Par-
liament till at last he sits around with nothing on
him but a Dead House scheme and an “ h,” they
would probably realise that their phenomenon
doesn’t amount to much after all.

person—but few care to try its effects on their
own charms. They need not, since Ayer’s Hair

Sold by druggists and perfumers.
Personal Items.
“Munro” Ministry is a misprint. It
should be “Munrope.”
Lady Jersey, we bear, has an article on
the stocks for a Sydney magazine.
Queen Victoria always carries her bed
along with her. So do most swagsmen.
Burdekin lias kissed Queen Victoria’s
hand. And there end his birthday honours.
An A.M.P, £5OO policy on Sir Alfred
Stephen’s life, issued in 1850, is now worth £lB6B.
Julian Salomons, Q.C., is the only knight
at the N.S.W. Bar. Judge Windeyer is the third
knight on the Bench.
Oscar Wilde’s wife belongs to the “Ra-
tional Dress ” Society, whose supporters are now
riding horseback straddlewise.
A son of Major Lockyer, the pioneer of
Albany, W.A., lives in quiet retirement, at a
green old age, at Rushcutters Bay, Sydney,
Journalist Comyns, about to. start a paper
at Matimuk (Vie.), asked Victor Daley to
suggest a title. “Call it the Natimuk-Rake,” said
Daley.
A certain wealthy young nobleman, who
not long ago visited Australia, borrowed, the
other day, £50,000 in London and paid £60,000
for it.
Railway-Commissioner Oliver commenced
life as a law-stationer’s clerk in the office of the
late Mr. Hemming, afterwards Commissioner for
Stamps.
Speaker Steere, K.C.M.6., was the only
man in a recent big West Australian crowd who
uncovered during the playing of the National
Anthem.
Kawau Island, the late residence of Sir
George Grey, has again changed hands at a big
profit. It is now the property of Mrs. Buxton, of
Melbourne.
They say that Gov. Kintore, during his
recent overland trip, could give most of his party
a start in pitching tents and packing swags, also
in matutinal horse-hunting.
The full name of the King of Hawaii, as
read on the plate of his coffin-lid, was David
Laamea Kamanakapu Mabinulani Nalocaehuo-
kalani Lumelani Kalakaua.
The quarrel between Bishop Smith and
Cardinal Moran about precedence, on Christian
principles, is, of course, as to who shall go last.
“ For the last shall be first.”
Who knows that after all Parnell may not
astonish the world by marrying Mrs. O'Shea’s
eldest daughter instead of Mrs. O’Shea herself on
the day fixed for his wedding ?
The latest LL.D. of St. Andrew’s Uni-
versity is Rev. David Bruce, a Sydney clergyman
who has been rendered no poorer by the decease of
the late N.S.W. Millionaire Berry.
Mayor Sutton, the hero of Goose Green
Gully, was returned at the head of the poll as one
of the members for South Launceston, the
largest constituency in Tasmania.
Premier Parkos, in his speech at Cooma,
said that his paper, the Empire, cost him £lOO a
day for three years. This, of course, means that
the Empire cost £lOO a day to produce.
Old Sam Cox, who died in Launceston
(Tas.) Invalid-depot the other day, was only about
06 years of age, and not 111 as stated. He was a
line romancer, and “ came out for turnips.”
What makes the rich Hirsch so liberal is
disgust with existence. His son is dead, and he
is alone in the world. His reputation is shady,
and the Rothschildren don’t mix their drinks with
him.
The blunt and fearless Colonel De Wolski,
whose exposures of official corruption in N.S.W.
are historical, has determined not to outstay his
present term of engagement as director of military
w„rks.
The newly-married John Hurley, member
for Hartley, (N.S.W.), is once more tilting
against gambling; he wants to suppress “all
games of chance.” Now, John, marriage is t
game of chance.

The Prince of Wales, it is cabled, is in the
habit of carrying round with him his own card-
counters. In Australia, when a man is arrested
with his own special gambling implement! in his:
possession, he is indicted “ under the vag.”

sion of Chief-Justice and Deputy-Governor Way
from the birthday “ honours ” list. S. J. W. Avas.
put down as a certainty this time, especially as.

The Argus’ notice of Sara’s first night was;
written by James Smith, and carried all readers
away by its splendid A’erve, but those who were
there thought it rather overdone. Some prefer
the careful notice written by Dr. Pearson for the
Age.
A nephew of Richard Cobden has just died
near Orange, N.S.W. The brother of the re-
former, used, in years gone by, to keep an hotel at
Molong—in the building now the homestead of
Mr. Betts, J.P., at Vale Head —and lie lies buried
in an unmarked spot on the banks of the Bell
River.
Mr. William Byrne, M.L.C., who was
born at Parramatta during the reign of the trium-
virate (Colonels Johnstone, Foveaux, and Pater-
son) that succeeded the deposed Gov. Bligh,
last week completed his eighty-third year. Mr.
Byrne is “father” of the N.S.W. Legislative
Council.
Mr. Jones, the Victorian hangman, has a
wife and two children, and lives, like several
hangmen we have personally known, in a neat
vine-embowered cottage. The N.S.W. hangman
has shifted from his bower of bliss at Paddington,
and now resides in an isolated spot by the sad sea
waves at Bondi.
Before Melbourne Royal Society, Rev. J.
H. Read read a paper in which he adversely
criticised the style of Rudyard Kipling and com-
pared it unfavourably with “ the purity of style
displayed by leader-writers for the Melbourne
daily press.” And mentally, no doubt, with the
elegant diction of his own ghastly sermons!
Miserable wretch !
At last Goodwood (Eng.) races, Whiles
joined Lady Dudley’s party and sat down on the
grass for luncheon. A few hundreds of grinning
onlookers crowded round to see the Heir Apparent
gnawing a goose-leg, and one joker yelled out—
“ Here y’are! This way ter see the Lion
a-feedin’.” The police charged the mob and
Tummy got away with his goose.
George Augusta Sala says, in a congratula-
tory letter to not the foremost artist in Australia,
or even in Brisbane, where the recipient now
resides :—“ Ever so many years ago, I was an
artist myself; and, old and purblind ns 1 am, I
hope that I have not altogether lost my love for,
and appreciation of, art.” Sala certainly possesses
one incomparable painting of his own —his nose.
A current mistake about Duncan Gillies is
that he rose from being an “ordinary” Ballarat
working miner. He was “a man of education
as Frank Clark sings—although he did work in
the mine with Peter Lalor, but Duncan was not
heard of in any way in connection with the
Eureka. Some friends gave him a start with a
big subscription, and shares in the Nelson mine,
which didn’t turn out much.
“A DOLL'S HOUSE.'
YOUNG WIFE; “ Good-bye, Toobald, it has just struck me I have been living all this
time with a strange-looking man, and I’m full up.”
TOOBALD: “But will you leave the children?”
YOUNG WIFE : “ Yes, they are strange-looking too. Ta, ta.”
Mr. Hurlbert, who lias failed to disprove
certain allegations of obscene letter - writing,
brought against him in a disreputable breach-of-
promise case, is the smart Yankee who professed
to have seen the original Parnell letter. The
Tories made a great show of believing Hurlbert’s
cock-and-bull story at the time, but now the man’s
character has gone—taking the mythical Parnell
letter with it.
When Tom Russell was N.Z. Minister of
Defence, says a Maoriland paper, a naval lieu-
tenant applied for command of one of the gunboats
on the Waikato. He was plainly dressed, and his
physique was not that of an Adonis, so Mr.
Russell said, “Well, you don’t look like a naval
officer.” His interlocutor calmly “ eyed over ”
Mr. Russell, who had as few claims to be called a
beauty, and said, “Well, you don’t look much
like a War Minister,” Russell shipped him at
once.
London Hawk says that A. L. Gordon’s
verses wei’o very poor «tuff indeed —a mixture of
sour Swinburne and bad Bret Harte. There is in
this perhaps a colour of truth, but, nevertheless,
Gordon wrote one or two purely original things
which, of their kind, a> e unexcelled in the English
language. Tell us of a better ballad than “Where
shall we go for our garland, glad at the falling of
the year?” Swinburne would not be half-an-
inch short of his present fame had he written
“ The Rhyme of Joyous Guard.” Robert Brown-
ing would have been a little taller had he been the
author of “ How we beat the Favourite.”
W. Kinnaird Rose, editor of Brisbane
'Courier, leaves for England shortly, and speaks
somewhat ambiguously of returning after a six-
months’ absence. Rose is a Scottish advocate
and a Queensland barrister, and is understood to
have been war-correspondent for the Scotsman
when “Fourteen-Flags” Maclver was serving
under General Ignatieff in Servia and Bulgaria.
„ Rose is a passionate lover of Burns and Milton—
rather a curious blend for even a Scotchman. He
is also credited with being deeply versed in
theosophy and esoteric Buddhism, and, when
patriotically engaged in preserving Hogmanay
from getting mouldy, he tells glowing anecdotes
of Madame Blavatsky, who has just departed to
thellenceness to see wdiet her her particular Nirvana
has been reached.
Says Mojicure Conway in the “ Open
Court”:—
In all matters relating to sex and marriage, Bradlaugh
was not merely chaste personally, but exceptionally con-
servative in opinion. Yet he was cruelly slandered in
relation to a refined and eminent lady. The gossip was
all the more cruel because, as I happen to know, these
two leaders of Freethought had deliberately sacrificed
their happiness for the sake of their example and the
honour of their cause. The lady had been driven out of
her home by her husband because of her heresies, was
legally separated, though not divorced, and left dependent
on her own energies for her own and her little daughter’s
bread. Bradlaugh’s wife had long been in an asylum.
Morally divorced, their union might have been pardoned
by society in any but so-called “Infidels.” Any intrigue
was impossible to either, so with love in their hearts they
continued apart to the last. In speaking of this matter
Bradlaugh once said to me: “If I know myself, I have
not a passion I would not crush like an eggshell rather
than stain the honour of ray cause.”
There are a thousand individuals in the
U.S. w’orth more than £200,000, while a good half-
dozen can boast of being the owners of £20,000,000!
The present fortune of the Vanderbilts totals to
the respectable sum of £50,000,000. Roekfeller,
President of the Standard Oil Company, has an
annual income of £3,000,000, or £BOOO per day. By
compounding this income at six per cent, semi-
annually, it will be seen that at the end of 25
years his fortune would amount to £160,000,000.
Astor’s income, which is even larger, would, at the
end of 25 years, amount to £175,000,000. Jay
Gould has £1,000,000 a year, so that in 25 years
he would be worth only £55,000,000. There are
36 individuals worth over £2,000,000, while there
are 87 more worth over £1,000,000. All of these
fortunes are, however, not held by men, many
women being high in the list. Mrs. Hettie Green
heads the list with £6,000,000, there being 15
others who own over £1,000,000.
St. James’s Gazette, one
of the howlingest organs of English
Toryism, wants to have flogging
re-established in the army as the
only means of putting down dis-
affection. The curious thing about
the Tory is that he always wants
flogging re-established in some
locality where it won’t affect him
personally. If flogging will pro-
duce a feeling of contentment in
the mind of asoldier, why shouldn’t
it lead to the same results in the
case of a Tory journalist, and if the
soldier's boss should be pennitted to flog him
when things don’t go smoothly why shouldn’t the
proprietor of the Gazette come down and
administer a breezy walloping to the editor of
that paper when matters don’t seem to be going
right ? Then the world would be edified next day
by noticing the difference between the views of a
Tory editor who had been whaled, and the same
individual in his former unwhaled condition. A
system of universal military service, with abun-
dant flogging throwm in, ivoiild convert almost
every Tory in England in one act—provided there
was enough flogging, of course.

Should Women Vote!
We think they should ! Because a woman
has more good, hard, common sense than a man.
Because she makes less bluster about her rights,
hut quietly maintains them all the same,
and much better than a man does. Because
she don’t give thirty shillings for an article
that she knows very well she can get for
eighteen. Because she does not stalk loftily
from the counter without her change, because
the shopman is a bit reluctant about counting
it out. Because she will hold her money tightly
in her dear little right hand for two hours until
she first gets a receipt for it from the fellow
who made her husband pay the same hill three
times last year. Not any “ Just give you credit
for it ” for her. Women have the most brains,
and by all that is brave, self-reliant and sensible
they should vote and run the government.
They have the hardest part of nature’s tasks to
suffer, they labour harder than men and more
continuously, and with less complaint. Let a
man suffer a little toothache and he will rave
and scream and turn the world upside down,
but a woman will suffer ten times the misery
without complaint. A woman has to bear ten
times the physical strain that a man does, and
it is only natural that they should be more
prone to weakness, debility, liver complaints,
headache and such simple ailments which
though, perhaps, not fatal in themselves are
liable to terminate in serious disease if not
promptly checked in their early stages. Thou-
sands of women have been rescued from suffer-
ing and misery by a timely recourse to Clements
Tonic, a remedy specially suited to the ailments
of women, being specially suitable for anaemic,
hysterical, and hypochondriacal constitutions.
A lady well known in Parramatta, N.S.W.,
writes under date September 10th :—“ Sir, —
Four years ago I caught a severe cold which
settled in my head and chest, and caused an
incessant cough and copious discharge of
phlegm ; this brought on great weakness and
debility. After considerable attention and
doctors’ treatment, the cough was relieved and
finally cured, but the debility remained and
seemed to defy the skill of the medical men and
the power of medicine. I had no appetite, and
could scarcely eat a particle of food, and was
almost reduced to a skeleton, having entirely
wasted away. I had taken pints of cod-liver
oil, malt extracts, and other medicines with-
out relief, when I was recommended to take
Clements Tonic. I procured a bottle, and am
thankful to say that I immediately began to
improve; I could eat better, and relish and
digest what I did eat. I gained flesh every
day, and my strength rapidly returned, and
after six weeks’ treatment with Clements Tonic,
I felt a new woman altogether. I took Clements
Tonic six months ago, and have never had any
sickness since, and am only too glad to add
my name to your list of grateful sufferers.—
Yours sincerely, Mary Kellett.” And Mrs.
John Taylor, 24, O’Connell-street, Newtown,
N.S.W., says :—“Dear sir, —This is to certify
that I have been suffering from neuralgia and
debility for the last twelve months, during
which time I endured the most excruciating
pain. I tried many advertised cures without
avail, until I used Clements Tonic, when after
the fourth dose I got relief and after taking one
bottle the effect was wonderful. I shall recom-
mend Clements Tonic to my friends as a good
and safe cure, and would advise others suffer-
ing as I have done to use it.” Clements Tonic
is sold everywhere, but sufferers must be care-
ful to get the genuine as many injurious and
useless substitutes are frequently offered for the
sake of extra profit by unscrupulous vendors.
Round the Shops and Show.
At this time of the year the hair is apt to
fall out, but, instead of wasting their combings,
ladies should send them to Mr. G. Van Brakkel,
82 King-street, where they can have them made
up, no matter how matted and tangled they may
be. Mr. Van Brakkel has also invented a won-
derful hair-restorer. It is composed entirely of
vegetable matter, therefore, in using it no fears
for ill-effects need be entertained. It completely
restores grev hair to its original colour.

There is only one City Buffet in Sydney
and any lady who has not yet invited herself t
dine there should certainly make a point of doing
so next time she is in town. The table appoint-
ments, serving and menu are excellent. The price
of a solid mid-day meal varies from one shilling
upward. Corner of George and Margaret streets.
Ladies room opens off George-street.

Society
The ugly religious “ precedence ” question
has again arisen at N.S.W. Government House.
Gov. Carington, following Wales’ precedent, gave
first place to Cardinal Moran, by right of the
latter’s “rank;” Gov. Jersey, it would appear,
declines to concur in this, and, like Gov. Loftus,
awards first place to the Anglican bishop. In
Governor Jersey’s private house he should be per-
mitted to do as he pleases ; in his official house he
should be bound by the fact that no Australian
State has any official religion. Nor should he, in
The Bulletin s opinion, ever consider an ecclesias-
tic’s personal “rank,” as “ rank ” is easily manu-
facturable by any sect in the race for the head of
the table. Official local seniority should determine
all questions of precedence, and should the head
of the Jewish Church, oi% for the matter of that,
the head of the Particular Baptists, be senior, he
should be accorded precedence. In any case, it is
most disgusting, considering Biblical example, to
see alleged Christian gentlemen squabbling over
such a matter.
♦ * *
The luckiest people in Sydney just now
are said to be the crowd interested in the Last
Call mine at Croydon (N.Q.), which, up to date,
is being described chiefly by the aid of the
adjectives erstwhile owned by Mount Morgan and
(in its turn) Taranganba. The new thing—based
on the effects of an alleged thermal spring—is
absolutely believed in by those concerned, and
their faith is fortified by the favourable opinions
of Mr. Mark Hammond, who has been there and
seen for himself, and of a well-known Victoriar
mining-expert. One or two of the people “in
it ” have of late been somewhat below financial
water-level, and if you had looked in on them
recently during the wet Weather you would have
found them moodily resting their feet upon the
office-table, while their coats were drying before a
feeble fire. But now the order of the day is tall
hats, cuffs down to the second joint of the thumb,
gold-mounted walking-sticks—and they tell you
the time of day with saucer-sized, gold stop-
watches. The holder of each original share (£l5
actually paid) is entitled to consider himself worth
£15,000.

The young lady, not unknown in Govern-
ment House circles, who, late the other evening,
performed an Irish jig outside a fashionable Sydney
restaurant, with so much spirit and abandon, to an
appreciative audience of naval and military
officers, is earnestly advised to go for the variety
stage. Johnny Sheridan will jump at her.
* * *
Last Saturday, after the dissolution of the
N.S.W. Assembly, an ex-member, travelling on a
Sydney tram, when asked for his fare lovingly
pointed to his “pass.” His next neighbour, when
asked for his fare, pulled out his gold chronometer,
mildly asking the conductor if “ that would not
‘pass’ him?” The “member” then had to
“part.”
# # #
A man came thumping at the door of
Government House, Melbourne, the other day,
and told the Jeameses to fetch out Lord Hope-
toun, as he wanted to ask that young man why he
had condemned him (the angry individual) to be
executed at 10 o’clock next morning. “ Can’t you
see I’m hopelessly insane ? ” enquired the man.
As he seemed a little that way, they said “Cer-
tingly,” and hid behind the door until'a policeman
arrived. A policeman always turns up just as
things are getting funny at Hopetoun’s establish-
ment. Only a few days before the condemned
prisoner came there was a lofty sort of lunatic
who buttonholed Secretary Wallington and de-
manded “the privileges and distinctions of a titled
aristocrat.”' “ What are they ? ” said Wallington.
“ Bed and tucker at the expense of the country —

He was run in, although there were letters of

While Jackson v. Jackson shows how a
spouse can kick up her heels, Stephen v. Stephen,
in Melbourne, shows how a Mr. Jackson, divorced
from Mrs. J., has to pay for her keep after she
has married another man. This is too low down ;
it amounts to keeping the new husband.
Coroner You], of Melbourne, observes that
“ if a man shot at everyone he found in his house
at night, the result might bo that he would even-
tually shoot his daughter’s sweetheart.” If they
were taking a last tender farewell in the dark, it
would be difficult to put a bullet into Adolphus
without winging Angelina, we fancy.

The Bulletin hears that a newly-married
couple have secured the recently-executed John-
ston’s late Ballarat domicile at a very low rental,
and utilise the chief slaughter-room as a bridal-
chamber. And if so—why not ? It is only fair that
Love should conquer Death for once in a way.

* # #
A certain Australian Tramway Co. employs
detectives—-technically known as “ spotters ” —to
keep tally of the passengers carried on certain
trips. The billet seldom lasts more than a fort-
night, as the first time the “ spotter ” reports a
greater number of passengers than the conductor’s
trip-slip records, there is an enquiry, and the
“spotter” being “spotted” by the tramway
employes, his usefulness is over; whereas, the
first time he reports a lesser number he is incon-
tinently dismissed as incompetent. The Bulletin
hears of a “spotter” who broke the record by
keeping his place for five weeks. He kept out
of troubled waters by stealing a glance at the con-
ductor’s trip-slip at the end of each journey and

# # #
Yet another stone has been erected in
honour of Burke and Wills. This makes the third
now in Melbourne —but why all for Burke and
Wills? The inscription tells of no one else. What
about Gray and others who lost their lives in that
ill-fated and mismanaged expedition which came
to grief chiefly because Burke was so execrable a
bushman ? The business reminds one of the marble
erected over one of England’s generals by his
grateful country (he died much respected, of old
age). The grateful country quite forgot to erect a
stone to the memory of the brave fellows who fell
in the charge that made his name.
A noteworthy trophy of the Salvation
Army died in Melbourne last week —a woman
who murdered another with a hatchet, 20 years
ago, was sentenced to death, but received a com-
mutation. She had no friends when liberated,
about four years ago, but the Army took her up,
and she became a remarkably useful and kindly
officer in a humble capacity.

“Fools back horses, wise men lay the
odds.” Proverbs of Solomons.
# * *
Alice Cornwell, so the cable says, is again
at work in London on the Largs Bay (S.A.)
Harbour scheme, but it is extremely problematical
that anything will come of it. Princess Midas,
The Bulletin is sorry to hear, has lost most of her
money through unsuccessful speculations. ’Twas
ever thus with women in business. Her first coup
with the Midas mine made her rich, and at once
all sorts of more or less doubtful Australian ven-
tures were thrust upon her by so-called friends.
If the Princess had stuck to mining—which she
understood fairly—it is probable she would have
come out right, but she dabbled in all sorts of
things—land, timber, electric light and newspaper
ventures, and dropped money in nearly all of
them. Poor Princess !
# * *
An old-time lawyer’s clerk tolls us a little
incident of the borrowing career of a certain dis-
tinguished (N.S.W.) legislator—who once waited
upon Mr. William Barker, a well-known solicitor,
and effected an exchange of cheques to the amount
of £lOO. The solicitor’s was, of course, met, but
the legislator’s was, like many others, returned
“N.S.F.”- The clerk was sent morning after
morning to make demand for the money but with-
out avail. The legislator went on a stumping-
tour, and in his first speech managed to speak of
“ his distinguished friend, Mr. Barker, one of
the ablest lawyers in Sydney, 'whoso advice in law
or polities I have always sought,” &c. An early
post brought the distinguished legislator a pleasant
note from Mr. Barker “ Dear , don’t mind
that little cheque; we will consider it settled.
Yours, &c., .”
*# * -
Munro’s proposal to enfranchise all rate-
paying Victorian women has been received with
much applause at Toorak, as is only natural in
the case of any scheme enabling every wealthy
Tory to magnify his influence at the poll by con-
ferring a cottngcVupon each of his nearest and
dearest family relations in consideration of their
solemn promise to vote hard aginst the working
man. Lovely woman requires even less induce-
ment than this to plump for the candidate who
seems most “superior,”, and has the highest
instep, the instep, in such cases being, perhaps, a
stronger testimony to moral rectitude than soft
white hands with filbert nails.
* * #
Five new barristers and eleven new
attorneys were “called” at Sydney Supreme
Court last Saturday. How these people will man-
age to exist is indeed more than a mystery. The
Court has lately struck off the rolls an attorney or
two for permanently pocketing clients’ trust funds
and there such matters have ended, though were
a layman the culprit the criminal law would have
been invoked. People are now beginning to en-
quire whether the Law —the State—when it makes
a man “ an officer of the Supreme Court,” should
not solidly guarantee his honesty—or make him
find solid security therefor himself.
# * #
The imprisonment of Ellen Jays for
“ fortune-telling ” robs Sydney of the imaginative
genius of five well-advertised and well-patronised
personages—Ellen Jays, Madame Cora,” Mrs.
Lacey, Mrs. Wallace and Madame Lennox.
Judge Docker seemed to be silently grieved
because the Act under which the prosecution was
brought compelled him to sentence Jays to a year’s
imprisonment for the picturesque offence disclosed
in the information, but hinted that there were
esoteric crimes masked by this ostensible calling.
This at once suggests the question : If Ellen Jays
kept a disorderly house and was a decoy of the
most infamous kind, why not honestly prosecute
her for her alleged crimes ? Why advertise for-
tune-telling as a superstition important enough to
be suppressed ? Like every other superstition, it
is a matter of “ blind faith ’’—which is interpreted,
according to taste, to mean eithcrblind gullibility,
or an obedient, unquestioning and conscientious be-
lief in supernaturalism. And the State is not sup-
posed to interfere with “individual faiths.” The
clergyman who tells you that under certain condi-
tions you will go to Sheol and accepts money for
trying to keep you out of Sheol is guilty,
logically considered, of a graver offence than is the
old woman who tells the young woman that she
will marry a young man with blue eyes and have
eleven children.
# # *
As a matter of human experience, the
broad rule may safely be laid down that every
woman of 30 years of age, who has had the oppor-
tunity, has, at some time or other, had her fortune
told (generally scores of times) by either profes-
sional or amateur diviners —professionals for
choice. Fortune-hearing is the darling supersti-
tion of the feminine mind, which can always
furnish you with a list of wonderful instances of
miraculous prognostications. Notwithstanding
the constant recurrence of the fair man who is to
woo, and the dark woman who is to be a source of
jealous danger, and the long journey over water to
be shortly undertaken, and the long separation
from, but subsequent reunion with the Apollo
of her heart—the feminine belief (we now
speak of all women, from the Judge’s
wife down to the tobacco-twister) can’t be
shaken; whether these portents are read in the
lines of the hand, or by the disposition of cards,
or in the sediment of a teacup, or by trance-clair-
voyancy, or anyhow, or nohow. The old heathen
auguries from the entrails of a slaughtered beast
were not more reverenced than are the modern
babblings of glib and*shameless fortune-tellers.
# # *
It is not worth while to attempt, and it is
impossible fully, to put down this silly feminine
fetish. With each prosecution like Ellen Jays’, a
fresh stimulus is given to the fortune-telling en-
terprise. What is required is that where the
“ dens of the mystic ” are veiled hells of vice and
vortices of entrapment, the law should fearlessly
prefer the serious charges necessary to deal with
the odious crimes said to be perpetrated therein.

They say now that woman’s spitefullness
is the immediate result of too much tea. It’s a
fact that non-tea-drinking darlings are the more
charitable, and that the cruellest scandals come
from the mouths of those who take no milk or
sugar, thank yon.
# * *
A Melbourne publican condemns the “jug-
trade ”as a source of misery and vice. He says—
“ If the publicans would unite and refuse to allow
so much jug-trade we would take the first step
towards a remedy.” That sounds unselfish—nay,
almost noble, until the gentleman goes on to
explain that the jug-trade doesn’t pay.

PROSELYTISM BY POST.
What sweet and pious guile !
This note suggests, almost
A new Salvation Style
Of Proselytism by Post.
Eor any one can seize
At once—it seems to us —
The possibilities
That it contains. As thus
You spot a man of wealth
You say, “ His sin is Greed,
His spiritual health
Is pretty bad, indeed.”
And, being poor yourself,
Without the least remorse
You rob him of his pelf,
All for his good—of course !
And with it start in style
A little business, say,
And, in a certain while,
Grow rich in your own way.
Then, through the post (it’s best)'
You send it back, you know,
But not with interest —
And write him as below :
“ Dear Sir, —This life’s a sham !
I was a man depraved,
But now, thank Gawd, I am
(0 sound the trumpet !) saved.
“ The coin you’ll find herein
Uf you I did deplete—
Repent, 0 Man of Sin,
Hell yawns beneath your feet I” 1
This ought to make him dread
The rich man’s future grim :
If, when you write, he’s dead—
Why, all the worse for him.
Thus you may when you die
Remark, though sinners carp,,
“ I’ve done fny duty by
My neighbour—pass the harpT”

One of the little Miss Jerseys performed
the ceremony of christening the baby monkey at
Sydney Zoo the other day. It is well, under the
circumstances, that the Governor, the li tie lady’s
father, does not come to N.S.W. with delegated
powers as head of the local Anglican church,
otherwise the monkey-christening announcement
might lead to misconception.

“ I am ill,” advertises a lady in an English
paper; “will anybody take my handsome, quite
new, dark grey velvet bonnet from me? It cost
355. I will sell it tor 125., or 1 will take two
quarts of cod-liver oil, a packet of mustard
plasters, and one hundred quinine pills in ex-
change.”
• # # #
During the progress of a now famous
recent N.S.W. divorce-case (in which no moral
misconduct is charged and the unravelling of the
Gordian knot is sought on legal grounds only) a
learned Q.C. repeatedly referred to the priest who
gave evidence as to the celebration of the marriage
as “the co-respondent,” much to that genial
cleric’s disgust. The Court smiled consumedly,
and the erring Q.C. excused himself on the ground
that “ he was not so accustomed to the atmosphere
of a Divorce Court as his learned friends.” Some
queer definitions were elicited during the trial :
“ Your father was a R.C., I believe, Mr. ?”
“No, sir, he was not.” “ What was he ?” “He
was an Englishman.” A female witness was
asked “ Whether So-and-so was a young man ?”
“No.” “ Was he an old man ?” “Yes.” “What
do you call an old man, then ?” “ Why, a man in
the prime of life, sure.” Another witness, who,
two previous witnesses had sworn, had knelt at a
font, cheerfully asserted the contrary. “Do you
know what a font is ?” asked his Honor. “Yes.”
“What is it, then?” “It’s the steps in front of
the altar.” The same witness swore that “he had
never seen Jones alive at any time subsequent to
Ins death, at least, not that he remembered.”
In connection with the approaching Laun-
ceston exhibition, at the head whereof is Joubert, a
prize of ±llO is offered for the best inaugural cantata,
not to exceed 100 lines, on Tasmania, and if the
successful poet be a Tasmanian, the prize will be
made ±ll5. The successful poet is pretty sure to
be a Tasmanian. The peaceful little island is
about the only place iu Australasia where every-
body has a hobby and is free to, collect grubs and
write verses. The busiest man iu the busiest
street of Hobart is always ready, in his busiest
hours, to courteously and exhaustively discuss an
original stanza or a new species of worm with the
most absolute stranger.
# # «
It is cabled that the British Admiralty
thinks it unsafe for the newly-built ocean-going
Australian Auxiliary Squadron to go to Australia
via the heaving seas of the Leuwin. That such a
thing should have been published in Sydney is a
ghastly disaster ; for almost every little boy on
the shores of the Beautiful Harbour would be
quite ready to sail his canvas dingy round Cape
Horn in mid-winter.

# #- *
The other evening a bank manager at
Toorak gave a dinner-party, and among the guests
were another bank manager, a parson, a building
society directoi’, and a sharebroker. They were
.smoking cigars, and passing round the flowing
bowl, and having a high old time generally, when
suddenly the host’s small boy gave a yell of
delight from his seat beside the window, and
vociferated “Oh, pa, there’s a p’liceman coming
in at our gate!” Then .a sudden coldness fell
upon the company, all except the parson. The
host nervously put the lighted end of his cigar iu
his mouth and swallowed nearly a foot of hot
ashes, and then he seized a small dog off an
adjacent chair and tried to pour it into his glass,
evidently under the impression that it was wine.
The other bank manager turned ghastly pale and
hurriedly put his burning cigar in his pocket, and
solemnly emptied a whole decanter of port on the
table-cloth. The sharebroker rose suddenly to
his feet and made an unaccountable break for the
door, but when he was halfway across the room
lie seemed to recollect himself and came back in
an embarrassed sort of way, and sat down but
missed his chair and lighted on the floor instead.
No one seemed to notice him, however. The
building society man seemed all at once to lose
the thread of an anecdote he was tolling, and
began to make strange and unaccountable asser-
tions. “It wasn’t the influenza, after all, as it
happened,” he said huskily, “ only a pol'eeman —I
mean only a cold, so I put my head in gruel and —■
I mean I drank the bed and sat down in the gruel
—that is, I ate a porous-plaster, of course, and put
my feet in the bank as security, and dep sited
the gruel to my credit, and next morning I awoke
in gaol—in a perspiration I mean.” Just here the
other bank manager began to combust visibly
owing to the cigar in his pocket but no one paid
any attention at all. Then the boy at the window
suddenly screamed again: “Oh, pa, it isn’t a
policeman ; its only a postman ! ” and all at once
the four men woke as if from a horrid dream, and
the sharebroker arose from the floor, and the
burning banker extinguished himself, and the
host gave up crowding the dog into his glass, and
there was a great calm. But they all looked side-
ways at the parson, and the parson looked hard
at the roof.

The Californian Indians, we read, used to
throw the boomerang. Professor Davidson of the

cellent specimens in the valley of Santiago. They
have not, however, been used in the memory of

* * *
A N.S.W. editor is wroth because folks
don’t rise to their feet and remove their hats in
public places when “God save the Queen” is
played. The Bulletin goes further still. It is
indignant that they don’t haul off their pants and
substitute kilts, in recognition of the “widow’s”
Scottish proclivities.
* * #
At high-toned English weddings it is hoav
regarded as the correct thing to drop that old,
improbable statement that the bride will “ obey ”
her husband, seeing that she has long since given
up all intention of obeying him, and the law has
decided that she isn't called upon to do it. She
confines herself, therefore, to the naked assertion
that she will “ love and honour ” her fellow-con-
spirator, though even that looks a rather sur-
prising announcement, and he adheres to the
ancient fable about endowing her with all his
goods, though he knows that he really wants to
annex her goods, only her father won’t let him.
Also, they both state that they will hang together
till death, which is altogether too unlikely. Still
those anomalies will doubtless be removed some
day, now that the reform has once started, and
sooner or later the agreement will be fixed on a
plain, hard basis. The sentiment and the astound-
ing snake-lies will be knocked out of the service,
and the solid facts of the case will be crowded in
ins! cad. Then the parties will simply state that
they intend to stick together, barring unforeseen
accidents; that they will love and honour each
other so far as the circumstances allow ; that they
won’t make any rash promises about their goods ;
that they will cleave together to a reasonable
extent, and that they will be oneas far as possible,
Too much metaphor and too little reliable fact are
just the things that are wrong with the marriage
ceremony, and when these are put right it will be
as good a ceremony as anybody need want.
* * #
The two principal actors in the latest
N.Z. story were a member of Miss Jennie Lee’s
Dramatic Co. and a barmaid who dispenses smiles
and liquors at the principal hotel in one of the
big cities of the South Island. He fell in love
with Hebe. She, everyone believed, was just as
“gone” on him as he on her. They became
engaged, the marriage-day was fixed, and numbers
of friends were invited to the breakfast. The
“boys,” intent on giving the fair one a good
send-off, lavishly furnished the hostelry with
flags and greenery, the ring was bought, the
parson waiting; but, at the last moment, she gave
out that she was only having a lark, and that he
was not the man the Fates had set apart for her.
He has since sworn a vendetta against all bar-
maids.
* * »
The Age truly remarks that the profession
of licensed victuallers condemns them to see much
of a peculiar and by no means elevated phase of
life, and leaves them quite unconscious of the
great forces which are moulding society. Yes,
they mostly consider that the machinery of the
world is worked by beer-engines, and their notion
(jf the popular spirit of the times is the stuff that
happens to be in most demand at sixpence a
drink. As for barmaids —well, did anybody ever
try to draw one out on such mundane matters as
Capital and Labour, or Land Nationalisation?
Hebe is not fond of political economy, although
on broad (not to say suggestive) principles she
prefers Capital to Labour. She is not a keen
judge of capitalists, mark you, but still she likes
the chappie who wears a flower in his buttonhole
and never seems to do much during the week.
But the seedier fellow, who wears no flower
and steadfastly refrains from doing anything at
all, is her heart’s best love, as a rule.
* * *
English society, according to the London
Star, has resolved at last to drop the term “ lady ’
for good and all, and the English lady of the
future will be a woman—only that and nothing
more. When will Australian “ sassiety'” go and
do likewise? The difference between a lady and
a woman is har’d to define; and somewhere in
between the two classes comes that indefinable
individual commonly described as a “female,”
who is generally too shabby-genteel to be a woman,
and too thin and-shrill and socially obscure to be
a lady. Perhaps the handiest definition of the
three classes is this : a lady is a woman that lives
in a villa, and has wine and a fat husband who
swears; a woman is a person who lives in a
timber cottage and chops her own wood ; and a
female is either of them who lives in lodgings and
has a tendency to religion mixed with cats.
Things are a good deal confused, however, among
all these sorts and conditions of humanity, and if
one of them can be abolished it would make things
a good deal easier.
# # #
Judge Manning’s (N.S.W.) judgment in
the Fischer bankruptcy case is to no inconsiderable
extent an indictment of Mr. B. R. Wise’s Bank-
ruptcy Act. It also contains a very graceful and
Well-deserved compliment to the plucky lady con-
cerned, with whom The Bulletin sincerely
sympathises in her misfortunes.
The woman’s cause is man's ; they rise or sink
Together, dwarf’d or godlike, bond or free.

10 THE ELECTORS OF NEW SOUTH
WALKS.
Gentlemen,- Sir Henry Parkes pledged himself
to submit the Draft Bill of the Convention for
your approval. Jiis supporters have wisely forced
him to abandon that course, and to appeal to you
upon an abstract idea—a mere platitude—“ federa-
tion or no federation ; ” an issue upon which
nearly all on both sides of the House are agreed.
He has sent one unfounded telegram to the Pre-
mier of Victoria that he had got the approval of
the Legislative Assembly to that unjust measure,
a measure fraught with danger to every great
right of the Australian people, and particularly
unfair to the people of New South Wales. Now
that Sir Henry Parkes is face to face with you,
his masters, put it out of his power to send another
imaginative telegram to the Premier of Victoria
claiming that you have also approved of the Dra't
Bill because you are in favour of a just federation.
Leave no room for shuffling. Get straight answers
to straight questions. May I respectfully suggest
three questions which every candidate should be
asked : •
1. Are you opposed to the Draft Bill as it
stands? Yes or no.
2. Are you opposed to the powers ovt r money
bills it gives to the proposed Senate? Yes or no.
3. Do you pledge yourself that any scheme you
approve of will be submitted to the people?
Your faithful servant,
G. 11. REID.

The Chinese in Victoria have decreased in
10 years from 12,123 to 8137, and the aboriginals
from 780 to 584. In 1881 there were in Victoria
9049 uninhabited houses; now there are 17,461,
and yet there is many a man who has no place to
lay his head.

The Foreign-trade press is sarcastic about

If disclosing a policy were everything, Parkes
would be an archangel of a Premier. He is never
at a loss for a policy to disclose. Disclosing a
policy is merely giving a political p.n., to be duly
dishonoured and sued for.
What is a true labour candidate ? The
candidate who, whether from the ranks of manual
labour or not, sympathises with the wage-earner
who has to keep a wife and children on less than
£2 per week, and who is prepared to make per-
sonal sacrifices to get him a higher wage-rate and
a greater purchasing power at the expense chiefly
of the monopolistic and hoarding classes.

candidate. —

For South Sydney r there are four seats
three of which were, until the dissolution, held by
strongly democratic Protectionists (Traill, Toohey,
and Edmunds). The Labour League has now
nominated two candidates which—should four
Employers’ Union candidates be put up, will lead
to four men’s votes being split among five, and
may possibly result in the ousting altogether of
the democratic party from the representation of
South Sydney. Some arrangement should be
made.
George Black, one of the parliamentary
candidates selected by West Sydney Labour
Electoral League, is an excellent, all-round
journalist, a convincing platform-orator, and a
close student of political economy. Mr. Black
has long been attached to the staff of The Bul-
letin, which tries to be modest when speaking of
itself or staff, but which, on the present occasion,
sees no reason why it should refrain from com-
mending to the favourable notice of electors a man
of Mr. Black’s vigour, ability and democratic
sincerity.
With that delightful cocksureness which
is his special characteristic, B. R. Wise said
recently that “ Nowadays the word ‘Liberal ’ in-
cluded freetrader, and they would no more call a
man a Liberal who was not a freetrader than they
would call a man a Christian who did not believe
in the existence of a God.” This reminds us that
John Bright once asked an American if Greeley
still “clung to the idiotic doctrines of Protec-
tion? ” Another American who had heard of this,
meeting Bright afterwards at dinner, found an
argument on tariffism inevitable, and opened the
ball thus; “Well, Mr. Bright, when I talk on
protection with a freetrader, I always begin by
admitting that I am an idiot.”
The prospectus of the Sydney Deposit
Bank, whose director is Mr. T. S. Richardson, lies
before us. This institution has introduced a
novelty for public use in the shape of deposit-
notes, which can be purchased by anyone without
giving name or address, while transferrable with-
out endorsement, therefore can be paid away or
given as security, while bearing interest at a high
rate during the time of currency. These notes are
issued from £lO upwards for any term at the fol-
lowing rates : 12 months, 7 per cent. ; 6 months, 6
par cent.; 3 months, 5 percent. But special rates
can be procured for longer or shorter periods.
The Bank on fixed deposits allows for 3 years,
per cent. ; 12 months, 8£ per cent. ; 6 months, 7t
per cent.; 3 months, per cent., and during the
last four years a bonus of 1 per cent, has been paid
to all depositors in addition to the current rates on
their deposits. This is the only Bank in Austra-
lasia that thus divides its profits.
