Jackson’s Second Wife:
A SOCIAL SKETCH.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
JagksoN had been crying on his way up to Tap-
kins’ house. But even without the evidences of
weeping, Jackson would have presented an aspect
of abject woe as he stood nervously an the edge of
Tapkins’ verandah.
Jackson was pale and thin and sunken-eyed;
and certainly looked 35, though yet barely 30.
But hard work and a disposition to make himself
miserable on the slightest provocation had fixed
upon him the mark of the additional five years,
which he did his best to preserve by never looking
happy—not even in a moment of forgetfulness.
He had come about his wife, and this it was that
made him hang about at Tapkins’ door undecided
and half ashamed of his errand.
He was helped considerably out of his embar-
rassment by the sudden and unexpected appear-
ance of Tapkins, who, in evident surprise,
exclaimed : “ What!- you, Jackson ?”
For reply, Jackson’s face took on a sickly grin,
which really meant nothing, and must only be
taken as Jackson’s apology for being at Tapkins’
door. For the next moment his countenance
assumed an expression of utter desolation, and he
blurted out:
“My wife left me last night; she went back to
her mother’s!”
Now, though this desertion of his wife had been
a terrible blow to Jackson, and had caused him to
give way to tears, for very shame, every time he
had thought about it—which was very often—
during the 24 hours that had elapsed since her
flight, it is a fact, that, as he made the announce-
ment to Tapkins, he experienced a manifest sense
of satisfaction in being able to impart a piece of
information, of such tragic consequence to him-
self. And he had come to Tapkins in his distress.
Tapkins was a short, perky little man, with
round protruding eyes, who bestowed his friend-
ship upon Jackson in a patronising kind of way,
since he (Tapkins) was a retired di’aper, and Jack-
son, a grocer, still carrying on in a small way.
Jackson had but recently married a second wife,
a young woman who had given up school-teaching
for housekeeping and the collateral advantages of
a ready-made family, which she doubtless felt she
was specially fitted to manage by virtue of her
experience with the children of the State. From
housekeeper she had been promoted to the position
of wife and stepmother, but it Avas evident that
the “ place did not suit.”
Jackson had brooded over his trouble all day,
till at last he could stand it no longer and Avas
obliged to commune Avith someone. So he had
come to Tapkins.
It gave Tapkins a positive pleasure to listen to
Jackson’s story. They had had a few words—■
Jackson and his Avife— and she, “getting nasty,”
had shamefully deserted him, leaving her husband
helpless, Avith four small children. It was enough
to make a man cry— or curse. And Jackson, being
a weak-minded man, had cried.
“ And she took the baby Avith her,” the latter
added in conclusion.
Tapkins had listened throughout Avith the rest-
less imj ati mce of a Avater-dog waiting for the
thrown stick.
“Well, now, do you know what you have to
do,” he said to the miserable, long-faced husband,
“Avhen she comes back —and your Avife Avill come
back, you needn’t be afraid on that score —just
you give her ” (it Avas terrible to hear Tapkins
swear) “ a d d good hiding, and tell her to go
back to her mother, or stay—as she pleases.”
There Avas more practical good sense in Tap-
kin’s cold-blooded advice than will, perhaps, com-
mend itself to the mind of the serenely Christian
reader. It Avas difficult to get the iveak and tin-
ner A'ed Jackson to enter into his friend’s vieiv of
what was the right course to pursue Avith the re-
calcitrant Avife, and more particularly Avhen the
question of its physical, not to say moral, practic-
ability came to be considered from Jackson’s
point of vieAV. But by dint of energy of manner
and an unbounded enthusiasm in the virtue of his
plan and specious arguments that made Jackson’s
self-respect the point in question and at vital
stake, Tapkins finally Avon him over, and ho
returned to his Avifeless homo a much more deter-
mined man than Avhen he set out from it, an 1
filled with a stern resolve to make his wii'a
“ smart for her little holiday ” Avhen she returned.
A Aveek later, exactly falling in with Tapkins’
prediction, Jackson’s Avife returned. It Avas
toward the end of the day, at dusk, that she
walked into the store, Avith her baby in L?? arms.
Jackson at the time Avas weighing out sugar for a
customer who stood by intently watching the
operation. The husband looked, if anything,
more sunken-eyed and pale, and, Avithout doubt,
more miserable than ever. His eyes were weak,
and red —but he had not been crying ; not since
Tapkins’ instruction to him as to hoAV he was to
treat his wife upon her return —should she be so
audacious or so repentant. That injunction
revolving daily in his mind, as a duty to be per-
formed, had given to him a firmness that saved
him from an occasional snivel. And he had gone
about his shop-duties, and Avashed and dressed
his children each morning Avith a quietness of
purpose and preoccupation of mind that Avas a
matter of constant wonder to his eldest child.
And now his wife had returned. She smiled
good-humouredly towards him as she passed up
the shop. He made no sign of recognition in
response, but only looked at her —he Avas like a
spectre standing in the shadoAv—Avith solemn
melancholy. His hands trembled very much, and
he fumbled most stupidly with the scales; and
when the customer drew his attention to the fact
that he was using the half-pound Aveight instead
of the two-pound, he smiled and feebly apologised,
and took up the quarter-pound in rectification of
his error, till he discovered this new mistake,
which he corrected again Avith humble excuses.
It was not for some minutes after the cus-
tomer had left him alone in the shop that
he turned towards the glass-door opening
on the little parlour behind the shop, and
into which his wife had retired. Within
that room a duty lay before him, not an agreeable
one, it must be admitted, to Jackson of all men.
But for days he had been nerving himself to it.
Tapkins’ injunction weighed upon him with de-
pressing effect as something that must be done.
“ He must teach his wife a lesson,” he kept on
urging to himself.
He was now at liberty to follow her into the
room. But he thought he would go to the shop-
door and look up the street. He did so with a
secret longing that a customer might be coming
along, and so save him. There was no one in
sight, however, so he was obliged to go in and per-
form his part with his foolish wife. He walked
hurriedly towards the parlour-door, trembling all
over, and his throat choking—though not with
rage.
H e turned the handle, and before him on the
opposite side of the room, Avith her back toAvards
him, his Avife stood munching a biscuit that she had
taken from the chiffonier. She faced round as the
door opened, with a smile—ready prepared—upon
her face.
She was not the woman whom imagination
would have pictured as being drawn by love
towards a man of Jackson’s stamp. She was
young and good-looking, with large, mild blue
eyes, and a decidedly pastoral figure, that is,
it was full, if not round. She appeared more
youthful than her years. Her face was that of a
great schoolgirl, but she was 24. She would have
been prepossessing, but for a look of evil temper
that expressed itself, when she turned her head
quickly, with an appearance of surprise.
“Well, Harry, you see I have come back again
to be a good girl,” she exclaimed, as her husband
stepped into the room. Then happened what
Jackson had determined should not happen, and
what perky, officious little Tapkins would have
forbidden in the most energetic terras. The wife
advanced smiling, with the baby in her arms, and
Jackson advanced, too, with a mollified smile—
and the two embraced; and five minutes later,
when the shop-bell rang, Mrs. Jackson said to her
husband: “Nevermind, Harry, you keep baby—
I'll go and attend in the shop.”
The next evening Jackson found his way round
to Tapkins’ house. When the latter entered his
front room he found Jackson sitting there grinning
privately to himself, and when disturbed in this
felicity by Tapkins’ voice he rose from his seat
with a broad smile upon his lank features, and his
sunken eyes glistening and blinking.
“What! You, Jackson?” was Tapkins’ greet-
ing. “The wife?” he added, with ironical en-
quiry.
Jackson nodded. “Yes,” he said, glibly, “she
came back last night, and we have made it up.” •
Tapkins seemed taken aback, then he recovered
and said heartily, “Well done, I am glad to hear
it. I hope you taught her a lesson ? ”
“ Well, I don’t think she will clear out again,”
Jackson evasively observed.
“ If she does,” Tapkins remarked patronisingly
in reply, “ remember my advice !”
Jackson returned to his house filled with con-
tentment and joy. Without doubt a wife, if she
wills, can make herself a power for immense good
or equally great evil, she can be an inspiration of
gladness, or source of the keenest bitterness and
sorrow. Here was Jackson, the smallgrocer,
HENRIK IBSEN (Author of “A Doll’s House,” &o.).
naturally a weak, nerveless creature, stepping out
on his way home as if he held the fee-simple of the
whole of the township in which he dwelt, and
why?—Simply because lie knew that when he
gained his home he should find a smiling young
wife comfortably ensconced in the rocking-chair
in his little parlour, or else, perhaps, tucking
some of the children into bed.
When after the lapse of a week, followed by a
fortnight, and then a month nearly, without any
display of her “tantrums,” Jackson concluded,
with much comfort of mind, that his wife would
give him no further trouble. In fact, on reflection,
he was rather glad she had gone to her mother’s.
He had certainly benefited. His meals were
always ready for him, and in every way his wife
now showed a cheerful concern for his comfort,
under the benign influence of which he began to
contest with her in rotundity of figure. Indeed,
Tapkins himself felt a qualm of conscience when
he saw the improvement in Jackson, and remem-
bered that he had told this same husband to
chastise the wife who had wrought the happy
change.
# * *
A month and more had gone by, and Jackson
was to be found busy in his shop. The clock
pointed to six, and he was waiting to hear his
wife’s voice calling him into the little parlour to
tea; as was her daily and regular custom.
That morning, they had had a slight difference,
so slight indeed as not to be worth recounting to
the reader, who may not have had a personal
acquaintance with the Jacksons. Such differences
occur in every household where there are a hus-
band and wife, though to be sure—and it is a
matter for sincere congratulation—all the wives
do not exhibit the peculiar temper possessed by
Mrs. Jackson. Many wives, it is safe to assert,
would declare the state of matrimony to be in-
tolerable were it not for an occasional “breeze.”
As it was, Jackson had acted the meek man, and
allowed his wife the last word, rather than “raise
a row;” and to prevent the impending catas-
trophe, retreated to his never-failing refuge—
the shop. His wife had bestowed a very ugly
parting look from out those white corners of her
eyes, but lie had not observed it, and was there-
fore unprepared for any malice.
The clock jogged along, five, ten, fifteen
minutes ; then the unsuspecting husband’s patience
Le:ame exhausted, and he went ins .a to see what
had upset the household machinery. He felt a
little taken aback upon finding his young wife,
with her groat schoolgirl face, seated calmly at
the table, enjoying the repast spread out before
her, and which he was in the habit of sharing.
His children sat around ominously quiet. _ Doubt-
less they had been forbidden to acquaint their
hungry father of the meal awaiting him.
“I think you might have given me a call,
Sarah,” was all he reproachfully ventured to re-
mark.
She lifted her large, mild blue eyes to him, and
stared. “You should have come in,” she said,
with a nasty inflection of voice; “you knew the
time. ”
The husband had no reply for such a speech.
His wife was too much for him, he felt, so he
meekly took his seat, and the meal was begun and
ended in silence. Then Jackson rose from his
chair, and walked across the room to the cradle,
wherein his baby lay kicking up its heels. He bent
down and lifted it up in his arms.
“Leave that child alone —it’s mine ! ” the voice
of his wife sounded peremptorily in his ears.
Jackson nearly let the child fall in astonishment.
What nonsense for his wife to utter! The hus-
band’s spirit was not wholly gone.
“ Well,” he ejaculated, though quite staggered
by this new move on the part of his wife, “ isn’t it
mi ?” but even to Jackson’s somewhat dull wit
the argument was too absurd to pursue ; and he
showed a lingering sense of his position by stoop-
ing again to pick up his child (he had weakly put
it down at his wife’s sharp bidding). But his
purpose was frustrated by the mother, who darted
forward and snatched it herself from the cradle,
striking the father’s overhanging face with the
baby’s foot as she swept it up in her arms.
Jackson affected a weak laugh, pretending he
didn’t mind. “ Have it your own way, my good
woman,” he remarked sweetly.
Possessed of the baby, his wife walked haughtily
out of the room, leaving poor Jackson standing
gaping at her, and trying to take in the situation.
When the door had closed upon her he began to
whistle, and then slunk out of the room and into
the score. Master here, at any rate, he began to
rummage about among the shelves by the aid of
what little light was shed frcm the parlour. By
and by he got tired of poking about, with no par-
ticular object; and as it had become late he
thought he would go to bed. No doubt, ere going
to sleep, his wife would come round again and
they could make it up.
The little parlour was quiet and deserted when
he re-entered it.' This was rather to his liking,
for he would then go straight up to his room and
he could speak to his wife as if nothing had hap-
pened. It was not his place to make the advance,
he knew, but rows of any sort he could not
endure ; they upset him and made him miserable.
He put the lights out and trusted to find his
way in the dark. This was easy, and he safely
gained his bedroom door and opened it, or rather
he turned the handle for that purpose.
“ Good Heavens!” she had locked him out of
his —their room, in her temper. He tried it again;
it was locked. Then he called out “Sarah.”
No answer ! Then he shook the door, and
called again—and waited ; then shook it again,
and held his breath. Was she speaking? Yes!
“Go away and let me get to sleep !” The words
reached his ears, muffled, of course, for she had
scarcely taken the trouble to lift her head from
the pillow, but the cruel, spiteful hardness of the
tones could not be mistaken; so Jackson retreated
in hopeless dismay, without another attempt, to
gain his room. He found his way along the
passage, and into the room where his children lay
asleep. Dreaming, perchance, of a world where
all was joy, and happiness, and smiles, and
harmony. He threw himself upon the bed, beside
one of his sleeping children, with the feelings of
an outcast. It did not occur to him that it was
his duty to go and throw his whole weight upon
his bedroom door, and break it down, and so get
into the room and bring his wife back to her
senses. But, then, he could not have called up
spirit enough to have done such a thing, had the
idea occurred to him. He slept but little that
night, and it was small credit to him that he rose
so very early in the morning, as to be able to take
his shutters down before his rival in the trade —
Chalker across the road —who made it a boast
that he always caught the first worm stirring in
H
Jackson procured his own breakfast, as well as
that of his children, for the excellent reason that
his wife was apparently indisposed for that duty,
seeing that she did not appear upon the scene, and
with the recollection of her conduct of the night
before, still fresh and keen in his mind, the hus-
band was far from being in the mood to lead his
wife to a better state of mind, or possessed of the
will to insist upon her behaving as a woman, and
wife, instead of sulkily acting the part of a child.
As the day wore on, and customers visited the
shop, Jackson’s face began to lose its sombre
expression, and his spirits likewise began to
rise, under the stimulating and diverting discus-
sions that arose upon the question of the “high
price for grain ” and “ extraordinary weather for
the time of year.’’ He had forgotten for the
nonce, whilst chatting with Frogley, the trouble
with his wife.
“ Good morning, Mrs. Jackson,” Frogley called
out with jocular civility.
It was in the afternoon, and Jackson had not
observed his wife enter the shop from the little
parlour.
1 ‘ Good morning, Mr. Frogley, ” she responded,
with a pleasant face, not looking at her husband,
whose eyes were fixed upon her with a bewildered
look ; as she held her baby in one arm, while with
the other she carried a carpet-bag.
She had to pass close to both men on her way
out of the shop. She glanced across the counter
to her husband, and gave him a hard, meaning
look.
“I am going off now, to mother’s,” she said,
with a smile intended to deceive Frogley.
“What, homesick already!”—the latter chaf-
fingly exclaimed; and turning so as to address
both the husband and wife, added, with a broad
laugh, “ They are all alike—the women.”
Mrs. Jackson made no reply, but hastened on.
Frogley evidently did not notice anything, for he
turned and watched her to the door, and then
observed bluntly ; “ Your wife looks well, Jackson
—what have you been doing to her ? ”
As the latter only mumbled out something to
the effect that “he supposed the old woman was
all right,” Frogley in the absence of any en-
couragement to continue that topic, or any other,
gathered his packages together, and took his de-
parture from the store.
Left to himself, Jackson’s spirit collapsed ; then
as if an idea had suddenly come to him, he rushed
round the counter, and ran to the door.
In the distance he could just make out the
figure of his wife hurrying along, her bag hanging
at her side. She meant to leave him, then, it was
no childish trick, that going out of the shop ; and
the remark. He seemed at last to realise the con-
duct of his wife in the spirit of the average, and
therefore, of course, human husband, for he raised
his clenched fist, and shook it at her. “You—
you—you she —you she-devil ! ” he gasped out,
after which he returned to his place at the counter.
It was only a passing burst of indignation that
had seized upon him.
Anyone speaking to Jackson five minutes later,
would have conversed with a woe-begone, sad-
dened and spiritless object of humanity ; from
whom, each moment, he might easily have ex-
pected a copious flood of tears.
Jackson behaved like a fool in his shop for the
rest of the day. He made the most funny mis-
takes, in excuse for which, when pointed out to
him, he said, in a devil-may-care fashion, “Mis-
takes, you know, will occur in the best regulated
families.”
As soon' as he had put the shutters up in the
evening, he made straightway for Tapkins’ house.
Tapkins must have seen him approaching, for he
opened the door before Jackson could knock, a
look of intelligence and expectation beaming
eagerly upon his little countenance.
“ What is it, Jackson ?” he said, extending his
hand ; “ the wife again, eh ?”
Jackson bowed his head in a forlorn manner.
“What! —you don’t say so?” Tapkins jerked
out, with happy inconsistency,
Then Jackson’s tongue fulfilled its office, and
he fervidly narrated, under a deep sense of injury
and affliction, the story of his wife’s second flight
from her home. Tapkins listened with animated
interest. He thrust his hands deep down into his
trousers pockets, and kept them there, and pushed
his little stomach outwards ; and when Jackson’s
story was finished, he burst in with so much vehe-
mence as almost to make Jackson think he was
angry with him.
“Now, then, do you know what I would have
done with a wife like that?” he said. “I would
have thrashed her.”
Which, by the way, was the last thing in the
world Tapkins would have done, or dreamt of
doing. His wife would have wrung his small
neck had he even hinted at such an outrage.
However, as Jackson had no knowledge of Tap-
kins’ marital status, the moral effect of his
language was in no way impaired in consequence.
“But what am I to do?” enquired Jackson
bluntly and helplessly, coming direct to the point
of his own real trouble and little interested in a
problematic one of Tapkins’ suggestion.
“ Do about it?” the latter exclaimed in assumed
astonishment—“ do what I have said, of course.”
And standing back after the delivery of this
short but pointed speech, Tapkins contemplated
Jackson, as if conscious of having sounded the
note of deliverance of all wife-deserted husbands.
Certain it is he did not perplex the unfortunate
man by the utterance of any useless phrases of
cant that might have come trippingly from the
tongue under such tempting provocation.
After some reflection, with his eyes cast upon
the ground, whilst Tapkins eyed him with con-
siderable interest, Jackson looked up and said
simply ; “Do you think it would do her good ?”
“You would have the best wife in the world
after you had done it,” the other replied promptly
and confidently.
Holding out his hand and keeping his eyes still
fixed upon the ground, and without speaking but
looking deeply abstracted, Jackson walked away.
Nor did he lift his head till he bumped himself
against Tapkins’ gate.
When he gained his little parlour once more he
found three of his children fast asleep, whilst the
eldest was awake, keeping watch and endeavour-
ing to act up bravely to the position of responsi-
bility placed in her keeping by her father before
he had set out to Tapkins’ house. At sight of
this, which brought home to him his desolate
position, Jackson gave way completely and cried
like a child.
A long and wretched week passed for him.
Each day he acted the part of wife, mother and
father in his home, tending to his children in all
their personal needs ; dressing, washing, and pro-
viding their meals. At the end of the week, feel-
ing sure that las wife would never return, he
penned a laboured letter to her—which circum-
stance he withheld from Tapkins, otherwise abso-
lutely in his confidence—wherein he begged his
wife to return to him and his children ; and he

would forgive all.
The letter was a pitiable specimen of maudlin
affection, qualified by meet, and would-be stern
reproof. She did not so much as condescend to
acknowledge its receipt. Yet, as each day arrived,
Jackson started with hope and expectation, at
every shadow that darkened his shop door. Had
his wife at any one of these moments walked in,
and gone up to him with the baby in her arms,
and a smile upon her face, Jackson would have
tried to look stern and forbidding, and the next
moment taken the child and mother to his heart.
’Lapkins’ behaviour now became remarkable.
Every day he dropped casually into the store to
have a talk with Jackson, and every day he ended
up with the enquiry—“Any news?” To which
Jackson responded by a solemn shake of his head.
One afternoon —a good three weeks after Mrs.
Jackson had left her home —Tapkins was in the
shop paying his daily visit. He was at one side of
the counter, Jackson at the other, neither speak-
ing, both thinking very deeply to all appearances,
but they had ceased talking for want of something
to say. A step at the door and a shadow aroused
both, and both turned to see the cause. Both got
a surprise. It was Jackson’s wife come back. Her
baby was in her arms, and in all respects she
might have returned after no more than an hour’s
absence instead of an absence measured by weeks.
She nodded with a frank smile to both men, as if
indeed she had only parted within the past hour.
Jackson was speechless, and he looked at his
wife with a vacuous expression, his eyes following
her into the little parlour into which she passed
with an easy coolness and indifference to the situa-
tion that would have been sublime in its assump-
tion only that it was genuine.
Tapkins got frightfully red in the face and his
round eyes protruded terribly with excitement.
He leaned across and looked at J ackson hard.
“ Well,” he said.
Jackson looked like one who looks and sees
nothing, then his head moved round as if seeking
something that he did not want to find.
“Take this,” said Tapkins, in a voice that his
wife would not have recognised just then, and he
handed across the counter a light, handsome
malacca cane; which Jackson took as he might
have taken a knife to kill himself with.
Amd then Tapkins wished himself well out of the
business.
“Of course, do as you like, Jackson,” he ob-
served as a precaution, pointedly disclaiming all
responsibility in the transaction; “it is for you
to act —perhaps it is going rather far.”
“ I know —give me the stick,” Jackson said, not
heeding the latter part of the sentence. Tapkins
glanced down at Jackson’s hand, nervously grasp-
ing the stick he had called for ; Jackson’s watery
sunken eyes followed the glance and became in-
telligent ; and acting upon a sudden impulse, he
darted towards the little parlour, and then, as the
glass-door closed behind the grocer, Tapkins,
became seized by an equally sudden impulse, that
carried him in an opposite direction —he fled preci-
pitately from the store.
“ Heavens—oh Lord !” he exclaimed to himself,
with a little laugh, “I believe the fellow means
to do it.”
Outside the shop he almost ran into an
acquaintance approaching from the opposite
direction. “Hollo, Tapkins, you seem to be in a
hurry,” his friend observed, halting.
“ Oh, no! —l’m just getting home, that’s all—
nice day, isn’t it?” And getting a few yards
away, he called back, socially,
“ I think it looks like rain though.”
Gaining his home he sought out his wife, whom
he gazed upon with surreptitious glances, ap-
parently having discovered a new interest in her
person. “If he has,” he exclaimed audibly to
himself. 1
“ What are you referring to, Thomas,” his wife
enquired.
“Oh, I was only thinking about something,’’
Tapkins replied with a start.
“Not .much, I’ll be bound,” Mrs. Tapkins
retorted, in a tone of voice that plainly indicated
habitual indifference on her part to anything
that might interest Tapkins.
Next day the latter received a visit from Jack-
son. The two men met, as two who had mutually
shared and passed through some terrible danger—
and come out safely. Tapkins’ face wore a sneak-
ing expression, which he tried to smile away.
Jackson’s air of triumph and self-restraint was
most unusual with him. He began immediately :
“I have to thank you for your advice, Mr.
Tapkins.”
If Jackson had brought a love-message from his
wife, Tapkins could scarcely have shown more
astonishment.
“It was the right thing to do,” he continued,
with an approving and intelligent nod.
“What?” asked Tapkins, uneasily.
“The stick —you know,” Jackson answered.
“ But I didn’t use it upon her after all,” he added
quickly.
“ But you meant to ?” Tapkins nervously inter-
posed.
“ But I couldn’t do it again for the Crown of
England,” Jackson continued, fervently, appar-
ently following out a mental scene and ignoring
Tapkins’ enquiry.
Then he told the latter all about it. “ She was
putting the baby in the cradle,” he said, “ when I
went in, but she didn’t look round—and that’s
just what settled it, Mr. Tapkins. By God, that
did make me mad. I went straight up to her.
‘Now, missus,’ I said, ‘this is for you,’ and I
shook your cane at her.”
“You have brought it with you?” Tapkins
interjected anxiously,
“She was down on her knees in a moment—
‘ Oh, my God, Harry ! —you can’t mean that!’ she
said to me, and caught hold of my hand. ‘ Very
well, Sarah,’ I said, ‘I won’t.’”
“Quite right, Jackson,” Tapkins observed,
patronisingly.
“Just then the shop-bell rang, and between
you and me and the cat, there, I was glad to get
out of it. When I went into the room again she
was crying like a child. ‘ Well, Sarah, you have
only yourself to blame,’l said. ‘Yes, I know,’
she said. ‘lt is my temper, but I’ll never leave
you again.’”
“What I told you, you see,” Tapkins inter-
rupted, conceitedly.
“But, I hope no man will ask me to lay my
hands on a woman again,” Jackson next said,
after a pause, as if trying to solace his conscience
for a not altogether exalted act of wifely correc-
tion, and when he had gone, Tapkins walked
about the room, laughing with that deprecating
inward expression natural to the man, who, having
advised a mean action, discovers that it has re-
dounded to his virtue and credit. “ Well —well,”
he several times muttered to himself, “/couldn’t
have done it—no, not if I had been asked !”
Three years have passed by since Jackson
administered the moral castigation to his pastoral
wife, of the mild blue eyes and great school-girl
face, yet, in all that time, nothing has ever trans-
pired in their household calculated in the smallest
degree to ruffle their marital harmony, or to lessen
the moral of the stick as a deterrent to wives of a
restless disposition.

In Brisbane police court. Lawyer : “ Ai’e
you a brother-in-law of Eustace?” Witness: “No.
He’s only married to a sister-in-law of mine.”
Parkes contemptuously speaks of Dibbs as
“a gentleman who has been twice Premier of
N.S.W., for 50 days at one time, and 75 at
another.”
Thomas Murphy, journalist and printer,
and one of the fathers of the S.A. labour-demo-
cratic party, died in Adelaide lately, aged 89
years. The deceased practically founded the S.A.
Trades and Labour party, which only the other
day put three of its members into the Legislative
Council.
An English paper remarks that “ordinary
women prefer ordinary men” and that “the
common female mind cannot be got to admire
genius.” That’s just as true of men as of women
Rumored that the Queensland Govern-
ment intends abandoning its big “ balloon rail-
way” from Cairns to Herberton, after sinking a
million or two in trying to run a line up and down
precipices.
B. R. Wise says : “The only men out of
work that I know are in the public gaols.”
Wrong. The men in gaol get more work than
they care for. Mr. Wise ought to visit the
Legislature if he would find the unemployed.

Let it be distinctly understood that this is no
scheme of quackery to draw money out of the
afflicted, nor is it any sort of a patent medicine
humbug, since there is not a grain of medicine of any
kind required in the treatment. Notwithstanding
this fact, it takes directly hold of the worst cases
of constipation, dyspepsia, liver complaints, head-
aches, heart disease, incipient consumption,
diabetes, and Bright’s disease of the kidneys, in-
cluding fevers and inflammation of the lungs, and
of other internal organs—not by attacking these
so-called diseases directly, but by radically neutra-
lising and removing their causes, thus allowing
nature herself to do her work unimpeded.
We now simply repeat that the new treatment
acts so radically and fundamentally upon the vital
circulation without the least danger to the most
delicate coiistitution, that there is not a form of
disease that flesh is heir to, which will not be
affected favorably to the patient by an intelligent
use of this method of treatment.

DR. R. F. STEVENS’ INDORSEMENT.
Dr. Stevens, of Syracuse, N.Y., was the first
M.D. to whom we revealed the new treatment —
more than 20 years ago. . In writing to a friend,
about a year ago, he gives the following :
* * * * * “Nearly twenty years ago in this city Dr. A.
Wilford Hall gave me privately a detailed history of a
hygienic treatment he had discovered and adopted some
twenty years before, and which he had practised con-
tinuously upon himself up to that tifne. The history
included an account of the failure of the health, the
reasonings and conclusions which led him to adopt the
treatment, his rapid restoration to health, &c. From the
time of his first mention of it to me I have been entirely
satisfied that it saved him from a lingering consumption
and death ; and that in the years following, as I have evi-
dence to satisfy me, its continued practice has so affected
the nutrient and eliminating functions as to give to his
physical structure a greater degree of solidity, or, in
other words, a higher specific gravity than that possessed
by any other man living. I learn that he has persistently
continued the treatment to the present time—forty years
in all—and that his present health and vigorous condition
may rightfully be compared to the ‘ bloom of youth,’
though he is nearly seventy years of age. The tests I
have made of the treatment upon myself and in my prac-
tice in the twenty years past, have convinced me that
great practical benefit may be derived by members of the
medical profession and others from a careful study of the
rationale of the treatment as discovered by Dr. Hall, the
details of which he proposes to give in his forthcoming
book. A debt of gratitude will surely be due to. him for
whatever scientific advances he may be able to unfold in
the line of assisting nature to ward off disease, and in the
restoration of health.—R. F. Stevens, M.D.”

Asa sample of N.S.W. Government
business, it is pointed out that the State sells to
a Mr. Joseph, at £4 10s. per ton, all the blood-
manure made at the Government abattoirs in
Sydney, and that Mr. Joseph sells the same on
the spot to the fruit-growers at exactly £9 per ton.
Further, that civil servants are employed in
storing, handling and delivering this stuff !
Roberts’ Hotel, George-street, Sydney,
situated in the very heart of the city, is one of the
most imposing structures in the capital, while for
internal elegance and comfort it is unsurpassed.
The drawing, ladies’ reception, and gentlemens’
smoking rooms are all distinguished for comfort,
while the lofty bedrooms are special features of
attraction. The cuisine and attention leaves
nothing to be desired, and visitors to the metro-
polis will find Roberts’ Hotel the most comfortable
house in the colony.
Dear Bulx.etxn,—ln a recent Bulletin tale,
“An Angel Unawares,” an incident is recorded in which a
journalist extracted himself from the clutches of his land-
lord’s bailiff by compounding with that gentleman and
removing his furniture to another house. As fiction
worthy of the name should at least have the merit of
truth in regard to essential details, and to caution such
of your readers as might be inclined to adopt the expedient
of '‘Creeve Roe” I would be glad if you would publish
the fact that Creeve Roe has vitally erred on an important
legal point. The law stands thus: —Any land.ord can
follow the goods of his late tenant to wherever he may be
enabled to find them within the period of 30 days and
distrain thereon just as though they were upon his own
premisis. Thus if I shift my furniture into another house
owing rent at my late abode my present landlord is liable
to lose his rent should the first landlord track me up.
Further, should I have sold out to an auctioneer and he
in turn have disposed of my goods, the landlord can claim
the sold property and leave the auctioneer and purchaser
lamenting. The sole exception to this rule applies to
time-payment goods, which the landlord can levy upon
only on his own property. Once shift time-payment fur-
niture and it is safe. Experto credo. Yours, Charles
Junor. [This letter, which actually presumes to give an
old and talented, we need not add experienced, Bulletin
man advice on the bailiff-question, is so presumptuous as
to verge on blasphpmy. “ The landlord may follow your
goods, it is true, but he has to find them,” remarks Creeve
Hoe.— Ed. B.]
The Victorian “ Freetrade Democratic
Association ” has burst suddenly from hollowness
and internal gas, and there is nothing left but two
or three disconnected apostles and a pamphlet full
of disjointed horse-reasoning. The association
broke up because it wasn’t democratic, and none
of its members knew what Freetrade really was.
There were about seven of them in their best days,
and they used to sit huddled together in the
corner of a vast, empty, desolate hall, and make
surprising declarations to each other about the
progress of their great principle. The memb 1 rship
increased 14 per cent, the first month owing to
the fact that a red-headed person of weak intellect
joined the society under the influence of a misap-
prehension, but that was its firs 1- , and last success.
When the cold weather came the statistics froze,
and most of the dogmas burst, and the new
member, who had hung on from a convicticn that
there was a horse-race somewhere on the pro-
gramme, grew discouraged and turned the whole
thing up. And at last there was only one active
member left, and he grew tired of delivering
exhortations to himself in the empty hall, and
stopped coming.
Sir W. C. Windeyer still continues to
suppress the list of causes coming on in his—we
speak advisedly when we say his —Divorce Court.
Now, how many causes, criminal and civil, have
been decided on evidence volunteered by people
who had seen from the press that a certain cause
was coming on? How many gross injustices have
been done by the suppression of evidence ?
There has been some discussion of late over
the choice of paintings for Melbourne Art Gal-
lery. Why must the gallery perforce be Eng.
lish? Looking at recent selections, it is plain
that pictures twice as good can be bought in Paris
for half the money. Paris is the art centre of the
globe, the Rome of to-day. The first picture (bj
Lumiuais) intheN.S.W. Art Gallery is French.

Tales of Old Sydney.
No. VIII.—Bob Warphy’s Kindly Deed.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
-The low, black hull of the Meander,
prison-hulk, Woolwich, was, on a September even-
ing in 183-, bustling with life and ringing with
sound. She was an inert and silent mass an hour
ago; now she was vivid with motion and noise,
lor within the hour she had received into her
’tween decks and the barred recesses of her hold
120 ironed convicts. From the metropolis about
80 had come, and by a coincidence there had
arrived at the same moment a couple of consign-
ments of like sin and misery from the rural dis-
tricts. The summer Assizes and the Old Bailey
Sessions were working harmoniously together in
the good work of freeing Merrie England from the
rabbit-poachers, the turnip-stealers, the pocket-
handkerchief-“ and similar hardened
ruffians; and between them those justly-lauded
Institutions of British Law had succeeded in
cageing, at the rate of two a minute, six-score of
the said ruffians on the Meander hulk as a
preliminary to despatching them to Port Jackson.
One of the coasting contingent is a short,
sickly-lookmg fellow of 35 or 36 years of age.
I here is a week’s growth of brownish hairiness on
his pallid cheeks and chin; his linen, or the frag-
ment of it visible, is encrusted with dirt, and in
that respect resembles his hands, which are
manacled —one to a chain, and the other to a
brother-convict’s handcuff. Still, unkempt and
unshaved as he is, he looks what, in the cant of
the Hulk Reports is termed “Superior.” And
the man who could look “superior” after a
seven-days’ tramp on the chain from an assize-
town to the shipment port would easily pass for a
gentleman when attired in clean clothes of a
respectable cut and freed from disgraceful sur-
roundings. Seven days in a transport-gang
would have made the First Gentleman in Europe
look like a costermongering cad, and there is some
excuse, therefore, for the failure of Mrs. John
Griffiths to identify her gentlemanly-looking
husband with the sickly and disreputable convict
to whom reference has been made.
It is between-decks. Hustled up the gangway,
hustled down the hatchway, the chained and
manacled group, eighteen or nineteen strong,
to which the sickly creature is literally attached,
has flung itself down in a tired, reckless, and des-
pairing heap to wait its turn at the anvil-block
where the “ land’’-irons are to be unriveted and
ship-irons put on. And the woman Griffiths,
pretty and well-formed, with the wholesome
aspect of the countryside lingering about her,
though the strain of a great anguish is burdening
her as palpably as does the child she carries in her
arms, pass by not perceiving that her husband is
among the huddled wretches.
“I don’t—see—him !” she gasped, with a tone-
less hoarseness, to the constable who was guiding
her in the semi-darkness.
The constable had been aggravated by the sud-
den inrush of prisoners. The old black hulk had
been empty of an infamous cargo this month past,
and here all at once, just the day, too, that he had
arranged to enjoy a long-shore outing, she had
filled up. No wonder he was irascible and out of
temper. British Law expected him to be vigilant
and punctual for the sum of one pound three-and-
fourpence weekly stipend, but there was no con-
dition in the bond as to humaneness or kindliness
of temper. He snorted in reply: “Then, you
jade, ’ow the devil can I see ’im if you can’t?
An’ ’ow can I waste my time ’ere on ye a-lookin’
for ’im unless you pay me ?”
“I haven’t anything, sir—at least, sir, not
much .”
“You’ve got ’miff to buy your dicky-man
sum’at,'l know, an’ you’ll have to fin’ me sum’at,
too, or off you go ! I ain’t no time to waste
a-lookin’ for hussies’ men !”
Trembling, the girl changed the arm on which
the child, a bonnie boy of two years, slumbered,
and tremblingly drew from her pocket a netted
purse. She was extracting one of the paltry store
of seven-shilling pieces which it contained when
there was a sudden movement among some of the
London portion of the newly-arrived cargo, and,
in a moment, the purse had been snatched from
her hand. The thief flung himself back into his
gang—and a jeer of derision signalled that the act
had been perceived by others and was aoplauded,
r “My purse, my purse 1 ” shrieked the woman.
The cry was unavailing; the constable, addition-
ally angered by the loss of the anticipated bribe,
knew it was useless searching for the offender for
the stolen article, and pushed the woman insolently
towards the hatchway “ ’Ere, get ’way with you.
Sarves you right losin’ your mopuses, a-takin’ up
my time to no purpose, an’ me so much to do.”
The woman turned and prayed to the quarter
whence the thief had emerged. “Oh, sir, who
took my purse, please give it me. I’ve nothing
to get home with, or to get food for my child ! ”
The appeal was in vain. Still, it gained the
object with which she had visited the hulk. Her
voice reached the ears of her husband, over the
infernal noises of the place. He was lying on the
deck apathetic, craving for the death which
would not come, and endeavouring to drown in
sleep the consciousness and the remorse which
crushed him with a weight more terrible than that
of his irons.
“Mary!” he cried. “Mary! Is that you ? ”
He pulled himself up by the chain and a staple
in the bulkhead. “Oh, Mary, this is good of
you—and what is the matter dear ?” He dragged
his share of the chain and the body of his chain-
mate with him, towards where she was standing
in the half-light of the hatchway. And at his
words, the girl, forgetful of her money loss, and
mindful only that her husband was there, whom
she had never thought to see again, turned to that
dear soul and embraced him. What to her was
it that a hundred curious glances were resting
upon them —that she could not be clasped in his
arms because they were iron-gripped—that every
tender word that welled from her clinging lips
was overheard by a score of contemptuous ears ?
She turned and passed her disengaged arm
round his neck. “My John! I’ve found you at
last!” And she fondled him, pressing the little
one to his face, murmuring the while soft love-
words. But—
“ ’Ere—time’s up ! Come along now I” The
constable so interfered-.
“Oh, sir, leave me just a few minutes longer !”
“ None of that, now ! I can’t leave you—there’s
others ’sides you want to see their aneas [men],
an’ I ain’t no time, unless you pay me.”
The mention of payment recalled to her the loss
of her purse. She had nothing beside its contents,
and now they were gone! Sixty miles from home,
and not a penny to reach home with —God !
But the Lord-Commissioners who, at this junc-
ture had something or other to do with the hulks
(what, in the plenitude of their wisdom, was
never precisely defined), had made a provision for
exigencies of the kind, and if she couldn’t find the
wherewithal to remunerate the Lord - Commis-
sioners’ representative, the constable, to wit, for
his loss of time in guarding her —“ Can’t leave you
alone here, missus; you’re too purty!” said the
leering fellow—well, she would have to go forth-
with. And, with scarcely a word of her love
spoken, and not a syllable uttered of the trust and
faith and hope for the future which she had
designed to bestow upon her unhappy husband,
she was being torn away, half-fainting, from his
side, when the constable remembered her wed-
ding-ring,
“ You’ve a fawney, ain’t you !” enquired the
constable.
“ A what, sir ?”
“A fawney—a weddin’-ring? Ain’t you a
honest ’ooman ?”
Too sore for indignation, the woman thrust
forth her left hand. The thin band of gold rested
upon the proper finger.
“ Oh, I sees, you’re right, missus! Well, jest
you give me that, an’ I’ll let you stay a ’ole hour
with your ol’ man. ’Tis against rules, you know,
but I don’ mind jest onst, as you won’ see ’im for
a long time agen, unless you go out to Botany Bay
yourself.”
The constable’s chuckle was contagious. Several
of the neighbouring prisoners laughed—laughed
the more heartily as they saw the officer, the
trusted Representative of My Lord - Commis-
sioners, attempt to pull the wedding-ring from the
Woman’s finger.
She drew back her hand, affrighted. “Not
that!” she said —“here, my shawl!—anything
but that!”
“That, or you go on shore this minit! ’Ow
d’you think I’m agoin’ to waste my time on you
without sum’at? Come, no nonsense, or
else
John Griffiths (real name), convict, under 14
years’ sentence for embezzlement, tried under the
style of “ John Jasker.” was as morally weak as
he was physically. Still, there was one virtue in
which he was not deficient, and that was the love
of his wife. He forgot himself in the moment
which succeeded the hulk-warder’s words. Linked
to the chain on one side, and coupled to his twin-
rogue on the other, with irons on his legs, he had
but one weapon of offence—his head. He pro-
pelled it forward, and caught the constable sharply
under the chin, thereby achieving two things—
the downfall of the officer, and a very bad “ hulk
report ” for himself.
And the last look that John Jasker, otherwise
Griffiths, had of his young wife and child, before
he left for the New Britannia of a Southern
World, was as she was hauled rudely up the tem-
porary ladder to the upper-deck.
She tarried as the constable pulled her to the
foot of the hatch, and cast a look of tearful affec.
tion to where her husband had stood. It flashed
through the inner gloom to the bulkhead like a
lightning streak into the core of thunderous
mountain gorges. Her husband saw her action
and heard her words, though she could not see
him.
“11l never let it go, John, never!-—not if I’m
starving ! When I can’t wear it, John, I’ll not be
—be an honest woman ! ” As she spoke, she flung
out her left hand so that her wedding-ring was
visible.
#• • •
In a month’s time John Jasker was on
board the ocean transport-ship and sailing away
from Woolwich to Botany Bay. With him went
that hulk-report: “ A dangerous character.”
The hulk-report was as simple a device as was
ever invented for driving a man, and a woman,
too, completely and entirely to the devil. If in
one of the hulks controlled by My Lords the
Commissioners, “a system of extortion and petty
tyranny went on by which the hulk-constables
oppressed the convicts waiting transport,” My
Lords the Commissioners were happy to inform
the Majesty of Parliament “ that their Lordships
were in ignorance of the facts. ” Which was not
at all a surprising thing, if their Lordships would
only have understood so. It would, under the
circumstances, have been rather surprising if they
had known anything of the matter. Notwith-
standing, a system of extortion and petty
tyranny did exist, one consequence being that a
man or a woman who resented the latter and had
not the means to satisfy the former generally
earned a vile “report.” In the magnanimity of
the Law, no record of a transport’s crime and
conviction was “ sent out ” with him. That was
to give him a chance in the new world. But
what never failed to go with him, once he passed
outwards over the hulk gangway, was a “ report.”
And woe betide him if when a tenant of a hulk-
plank he had shown an independence of spirit or
a complete emptiness of purse.
“ Constable, ” asked the Hulks Superintendent
as he was compiling reports a day or two before
the Ocean dropped alongside k« take im her cargo.
“John Jasker, No. 7 mess?”
“A werry bad ’un, your Honor—werry violent
an’ insubordinet, sir. Saluted me onct, your
Honor ’ll remember—dark cell seven days.”
“ Yes, I remember. That’ll do. Next!” And
while the constable was recalling the “next’s”
conduct, the Hulks Superintendent penned three
words against John Jasker’s name: “A danger-
ous character.”
Such was the routine for which Mjr Lords were
responsible, but of which they were in refreshing
ignorance.
# # #
It clung to Jasker always, did that report.
It won him classification during the daily exer-
cise-hour with a crew of villains which included at
least two murderers and a resurrectionist. It
procured for him the privilege of specially-
weighted irons, and the scarcely more desirable
enjoyment of peculiar exhortations from the
religiously-disposed Surgeon-Superintendent. And
it docked his water-allowance by a quantity equal
to one pint per week.
It clung to him on arrival at Port Jackson. He
was sullen and desperate when the clerks from the
Principal Superintendent’s Office came down to
check-off the manifest and to ascertain the parti-
culars of the convicts, The Surgeon-Superin-
tendent could supply them with no further
details respecting each man than -were endorsed
in his Hulk-report except such additional
character as the transport had gained for good or
ill during the voyage. In Jasker’s case this latter
record was simply : “Bad.”
With a “ dangerous ” Hulk-report, and a “bad”
ship-report, and a lowering sullenness of the
features when the clerks addressed him, it was
only to be expected that these gentlemen were
prejudiced against him, and that on their report
he was not put down for assignment, but was
merely ordered into the gaol-gang.
• # •
He was still in the gaol-gang when, four
years later, his wife with her boy, now a sturdy
youngster of nearly seven, arrived in Old Sydney.
By infinite pains and by surpassing economies,
the woman, moved by that intensity of unselfish
devotion which inspires so many of her sex to
heroic exertions for what in the world's sight is a
trivial object, had earned and saved money enough
to procure her and her child’s passage to Aus-
tralia. She had expected to have found her
husband free. In the one letter which he had
sent home, he had told her he was in a fair way
to obtain his liberty as a ticket-of-leave, and was
not very uncomfortable. His statement was a lie,
but its motive was to ease her anxiety on his
account, and hence we may not condemn it. But
when she arrived and found that he was still in
the gaol-gang, condemned thereto by sentence after
sentence for insubordination and insolence, sundry
scowlings and shruggings of shoulders, were so
called, it was a shock to her. All the greater, of
course, that she had looked to receive a free
or partially free man’s welcome. Permitted to
speak to him once, he informed her that unless by
influence of a more or less honourable kind she
could obtain a pardon for him, he would have to
serve another five years_ for his “ticket.” That
had been the effect of his later sentences—and of
the hulk-report.
It was his hulk-report always. “What is the
pris’ner’s police-report? ” a magistrate would ask
as Jasker was presented before him. “ Bad, y’r
worship ! ” the prosecuting officer would reply.
And then would he begin the repetition of Jasker’s
offences from his endorsed parchment, with the
hulk-report always heading the list. A “ good ”
hulk-report counted for little with any magistrate;
a bad one for much whether or not it was con-
firmed by subsequent conduct. And Jasker’s
later conduct had been marked by repeated acts of
insolence and so forth. A “bad hulk” stamped
the whole after-prison career of nine out of ten
convicts who were unfortunate enough to earnest.
In Jasker’s case it had prevented his assignment
and postponed the issue of his ticket. The greed
and malice of an illiterate hulk-constable had
ruined the transport’s nature. It would have
been a tragic if it had not been so commonplace
an incident of the System.
With her husband powerless to aid her, with a
growing boy hungry for food, what was Mrs.
Jasker or Griffiths to do ? Free from the tie of the
lad, she could have earned a living for herself, but
the necessity of earing for the boy handicapped
her. Free seamstress’ work, with so many con-
vict women available, was naturally ill-paid, and
many a week, after the cost of her material and
the rent of her poor room were defrayed from her
earnings, the remainder barely sufficed to find
food for the boy, let alone for herself. At last
someone of her few employers suggested she
should apply for her husband’s assignment to her-
self, and promised to back up her application. It
was refused by the Board of Assignments ; “appli-
cant had apparently no means of supporting the
prisoner applied forand besides, “ the prisoner
applied for is of very bad character, his police
report being notoriously bad, and it is essential to
his reformation and the protection of the com-
munity that he should be kept under strict super-
v sion in the gaol-gang.” That settled it for a
time.
Then she got a hint from a junior in the Board’s
office that if she could find £lO to fee himself and
one Timothy Pounce, the matter of the assign-
ment could be arranged. They would make the
terms very easy—would only ask for half the sum
down and the balance in six months after her hus-
band’s assignment. Pounce, for his half of the
money, would forge an application from an (ficti-
tious) inland settler, and he, himself, would put
the necessary papers through the Principal
Superintendent’s Office and the Board’s Office.
Mary J asker, however, could not raise ten shil-
lings, let alone ten pounds, and the liberal offer
of the Board’s Junior Clerk was reluctantly
refused.
She was at her wits’-end when Mr. Robert
Warphy introduced himself to her. Mr. Warphy
—albeit he was a Treasury official—was not above
doing a kindly deed, and being by chance present
one day in the Principal Superintendent’s office
when she had called to importune that high
authority to grant her husband’s services to save
her and her boy from starving, he had been
interested by her manners and her still pretty
face. “ Who’s that woman ?” he had asked of a
Prim Sup. ’s clerk.
“She wants to get her husband assigned to
her.”
“ Are you going to grant him ?”
“No—there’s an objection—she can’t support
him .”
“S’posenot. What should a woman like her
want her man assigned for except to support
her ?”
“ Well, that’s not all. The pris’ner’s of bad
record. Very bad hulk report, and a dozen or so
insubordination convictions.”
. “ Oh, that’s it, is it! Well, she’s still a pretty
piece of goods, and it might be worth while helping
her. ”
“ You’re too good, Warphy, you are ! ” laughed
the other. “ You want another chire amie so
soon ? Why it’s only a couple of months since
you got the Lynn woman her huskararl’s freedoms
isn’t it?”
“ Yes, her and him. \ caught a Tartar in
each of them, but this little woman’s different,
I’ll wager. She’ll keep open bouse for me for
longer than a couple of months if I do anything
for her.”
From which observations may be inferred the
nature of Mr. Robert Warphy’s kindly intentions.
• * *
He called on Mrs. Jasker, otherwise Grif-
fiths, at her miserable lodgings in Kent-street.
“You are Mrs. Jasker, ma’am?” He bowed
courteously, for he was the very pink of affability
was Bob Warphy, of the Treasury.
She curtsied assent, as she, dropping her sewing
prepared a chair for her visitor.
“ And you wish, I’m told, to get your husband
assigned to you—he’s in the gaol-gang now. ”
Oh, sir, I do ! I can’t make a living for my-
self and my boy, sir, unless ” —she stopped, the
tremors of her starved frame revealing at once her
weakness and the excitement of the hope which
had already leapt up within her, for she felt that
this gentleman’s call meant that something w^qld
“ Unless he is freed ?”
“Yes, sir, that is it. Oh, sir, will you get his
pardon or a ticket for him. If you only knew how
we—that is, my little boy, sir, suffers from want
of food and clothes !”
‘‘ I can understand !” he said, sympathetically,
but never swerving one jot’s breadth from his
purpose. The Satyr is always relentless. “Well,
I think I can get him his pardon—or, at least, his
assignment to you.”
“Oh, sir—God bless you* sir ’’she broke into
a rush of tears. “ Oh, sir, how can I thank you?’*
“Oh, well, you know,!’ he said, “these little
services are always paid for by an equivalent!”
“ Pay—equivalent ! ” she repeated the words
aimlessly. How could she pay aught ? And the
tears came again, this time the acrid drops of
disappointment.
“ Oh, yes—you know it’s always done when a
Woman wants her husband’s freedom. Why, I’ve
had three women when it was only a case of
tickets, not of pardon—but seeing as it is for you,
dear —and that you’re young and pretty ’’ —he
stooped to kiss her—“ I’ll get him his pardon.”
Till this moment, his meaning was not clear to
her. Now, there could be no mistaking it. She
pushed him away with her utmost strength,
and threw at him a glance in Which there was
hatred of the suggestion and scorn of the man who
uttered it.
“I thought you were—a gentleman!” With
her thin hands beating down the tumult in her
breast, she forced herself to speak. “You take—
advantage—of my cruel necessity—of my starving
child !”
“ H’ay nothing of the sort! It’s purely a
matter of bus’ness—done every day here —quite
the regular thing ! You Want your husband and
I want —Well —you !”
“ Sir, my husband shall die in chains first, and
my boy and lin the gutter. Go, sir, go !”
‘ Oh, well, if you think better of my proposal,
you can always hear of me at the Treasury—
Warphy’s my name—Mr. Robert W-ar-phy !” He
turned to go, but paused on the threshold to say
one parting word.
“ And, look here—l’ll oppose any assignment
and any pardon—application for which doesn’t
pass through my hands !”
“ I shall go to the Governor, sir ”
“ Do, my good woman, and tell him what I’ve
said. Do you think he’ll believe, you—you,-a
transport’s wife ? _ Take care he doesn’t imprison
you for blackmailing and slandering an officer of
the Government. That’s my advice, and good
day; !”
That was his advice—the advice of a thoroughly
experienced man of the Old Sydney world. A.nd
she knew enough of Old Sydney to act upon it.
* # *
With breaking heart she struggled on fox
a few weeks longer, when her boy cried for food.
Then, one day, taking her boy for a walk in the
Racecourse, from whence they passed by the, as
yet, roofless walls of St. Mary’s Chapel to the
Domain, she saw her husband ih the chain-gang
working by the Wooloomooloo embankment.
She drew as near as she could to him for fear of
the guards—to speak to the men of the iron-gangs
was a misdemeanour punishable by imprisonment
- and, scourging herself into unnatural calmness,
asked how he did.
In a brief whisper, that seemed, so acute was
its pain, to have sucked its bitterness from his
entrails, he begged her to get him “out of that.”
“ I shall die else —get me free—anyhow.”
Stunned, she had but one thought. That her
husband had learnt of the ways in which Freedom
could be bought and sold, and that he wished her
to tread that path of humiliation and sorrow.
“ How could he ! ” she thought. “If I had been
he, I’d have died first!” But Jasker.did not
know; his appeal had been simply wrung from
him by the utter misery of his condition.
From the Domain she went to the Treasury and
asked for Mr. Robert Warphy. She was shown
into his room.
“If you will gain my husband's pardon, sir—
when you will ! ” She looked into his face urn
blushingly. It was not for her to blush, she
thought. If the transaction was nefarious, it was
still a business one according to Old Sydney
notions, and she at least considered she was not
free of its infamy.
• • #
There was this much to be said for
Warphy. He was a man of his word. Other,
men of the System had entered into similar bar-
gains, and they had not invariably carried out
their part of the contract. Very religiously
Warphy, however, did. Within a few days, John
Jasker, per Ocean, received a conditional pardon,
Warphy did not do things by halves.
On the night of his release, Jasker sat in his
wife’s little room, and moaned out to her his
thankfulness that she had succeeded in winning
his pardon.
“But you have not told me, Mary, how you
managed it! You had no money, I know ! ”
For answer, she lifted her left hand to the lino
of his vision. He looked up wonderingly, for he
did not guess her meaning. She had to make it
plainer.
“Do you not remember, John, my last words
on board the hulk ? ”
“That I do, wife ! They’ve saved me many a
time from despair, and all that despair means ip
the gangs. ”
“ I said that I’d never take my ring off so long
as I was an honest woman ! ”
“ You did ! ” And he looked at the hand again
—and saw the ring was not on it!
She passed to the mantel, and taking a tiny
packet wrapped in paper from it, unfolded the
sheet, and held out to him her wedding-ring.
Then he understood; and insisted upon the
whole story being told him.
* * *
On the next day, he earned ten shillings,
and on the next day but one, he went into
Simpson’s hardware stores, opposite the old gaol
in Lower George-street and bought a horsewhip.
TBbe ’Brearswy was in Geerge-street als®. Thither
from the store he passed, bat Mr. Warphy was
out—had gone to the Club in Bent-street. Thither
then he went.
And on the steps of the Club-house he saw a
number of gentlemen standing.
* * *
He went up and saluted them. One and
all stared at his effrontery, for “ Lag”’ was written
if not on his face, at least in the dragging of his
left leg. They were amazed to the degree of
wrathfulness, as, not content with saluting, he
addressed them :
“May I ask if either of you gentlemen is Mr.
Robert Warphy?”
“Ha, ha, Mr. Warphy,” condescended to jocu-
larly remark his Excellency General Ralph Dar-
ling. “This the usual stamp of your Club-
callers ?”
“He, he—not quite, your Excellency,” re-
sponded Warphy, annoyed. “If you want to
see me, my man, you had better sail at the
Treasury !”
“This is a better plaae than the Treasury, yopj
Honor. You did me a kindness, Mr. Warphy, dr
wife one—and a kindness done to my
wife is one to me.”

“ Tush, my man, I don’t know you, and if I did
f our wife a kindness, it is—he-he ! —I trust what
’m ready to do to anyone. ”
“ But, sir, this kindness is an exceptional one—
and should receive exceptional thanks. And, sir,
this is how I thank and repay you !”
Uttering the last words, Jasker stepped for-
ward and, with the whip, struck Warphy sharply
across the face, and as sharply repeated the blow
before the bystanders could arrest his arm.
“ That’s how I repay the kindness of the man
who obtained my free'dom in exchange for the
debauching of my wife !”

hour he was arraigned, and his police record —in-

ness of his nature mastered his'resolution. He
died.
PRICE WARUNG.
A RAY OF SATISFACTION.
I love my Lottie dearly,
Though ’tis now a year, or nearly,
Since first I ’gan a»wooing on a chilly Autumn
night;
That she likewise loves sincerely
Is apparent rather clearly—
But she has another fellow whom I wouldn’t care
to fight.
I hate this low intruder :
He declares as how he “ knew’d her ”
Before I came across the little beauty on the
Block,
And nothing could be ruder
Than the way he has pursued her
Since he parted us one night with an ungentle-
manly knock.
In a manner gay and dashing
Luscious Lottie I was mashing,
And administering kisses to her lips so ripe and
red,
When my nose he started smashing,
And a lot of lights came flashing
In my eyes, quite unexpected, till I fancied I was
dead.
I yelled, but on the spot he,
With a sudden grip garrotty,
Very solemnly suggested that I’d better soon
retreat ;
I was silent, sick, and “ dotty ’
As he skipped away with Lottie—
And left me with a sense of having suffered a
defeat.
I am constantly complaining _
Of the way that chap’s behaving,
But it never seems to matter, for he doesn’t care a
cent;
I always have to cave in
To that prototype of Slavin,
And he’s “taking out” my darling with her
family’s consent.
I throw her tender glances
When he gives me any chances,
Yet it’s only now and then I’ve opportunities to
to look ;
And while I’m weaving fancies
On the scene my rival prances,
And with a cry of agony I softly take my hook.
My love is true and tender
For the sweetest of her gender,
Although my cup of sorrow runneth over at the
rim;
If Lottie, lithe and slender.
Weds this muscular pretender
There’ll be comfort in reflecting that I’ve got the
laugh of him. •
The Magazine of Art for April prints an
attack on the infamous Sydney “Post-office
carvings.”
A Melbourne housemaid lately went to
her master and declared there was a burglar in
the house. “Pooh, pooh !” said the boss; “it’s
only your follower,” and the girl was so indignant
that she gave notice on the spot. Meanwhile the
burglar stripped the hall of all the coats and
umbrellas.
Premier Parkes says the Queen has con-
ferred upon him “the highest honours in her
gift short of the Garter.” Now peerages, P.O.’s,
“The Bath,” “Thistle,” “St. Patrick,” and
“ Star of India,” are all above the tawdry Order
of Michael and George, which was only instituted
** to oblige crawling colonials.”

Panton, P.M., of Melbourne, the other day
dismissed a charge against a sailor of having
wilfully broken a pane of glass, after asking
prosecutor if he really meant to allege that the
sailor “ ivilfully fell through his window.”
Melbourne Corporation fixes on electric-
lighting. Prime cost, £75,000 ; annual cost,
£20,000, —just about the same as gas, but to put
the wires underground would involve £400,000.
Only two of the original Victorian police-
force are in harness —Inspector Pewtress and
Sergeant Manson. Both date back from 1852.
Manson has been Sergeant for over 25 years. He
is a steady, well-educated man, and has had the
satisfaction of seeing dozens climb over his head.
Boehm’s Jubilee caricature of the Queen’s
head is to be replaced by something more lifelike
on the gold and silver coinage. Perhaps they will
give the job to a radical British sculptor, capable
of producing the image of a singularly disagree-
able, not to say sour-looking, old lady, without
any neck worth mentioning.

Mr. Herbert Gladstone, M.P., was on Saturday evening
initiated a member of the Friendly Drop Lodge at the
Barleycorn Inn, Armley. During the evening the hon.
gentleman was also present at an organ-opening in con-
nection with the United Methodist Free Church, Green-
side.
Good old modern mixture. Cloves atid cant.
Friendly Drop and Free Church. Drink and
doxology. Pewters and progress. Swizzle and
psalms. Graduating as a G. O.M., we suppose.

Tom Glassey, M.L.A. for Bundamba (Q.),
advocates the election of Judges by the people.
Tom is the typical working-man’s member in
Queensland, but he has managed to do very well
out of politics. Two of his sons have been
“ placed ” in the Civil Service since he blossomed
into a Parliamentarian.
It is cheering to note that the Australian
census is a failure as regards religion. The whole
column is a trick, as in England, by certain
dominant sects to sweep into their net a host of
people who really belong to no church. The census-
takers appear to have been sent out without any
definite instructions, and have failed to probe
people properly on religious details.
The Adelaide branch of the A. N. A.—like
a good many other branches —is slowly dying, and
the local authorities, who see that something must
be done, think of merging it into an Australian
Patriotic League to advocate Australian Federa-
tioh : all Australians, native-born or not, to be
admitted. The “Australian native,’ i.e., the
A.N. A., seems to be exotic outside of Victoria.
The U. S. going to interfere in Chili.
Right. Pan-America. You will see, if you live
as long as Parkes, a glorious Confederation of
America, beginning with a close-up of all the
South, under Brazil and Chili, after which the
North will whelm, with its U.S., Canada and
Mexico, for naught can resist the all-conquering
Union, which alone should have 500 million popu-
lation in 1991.
Whoever had the management of Sir
George Grey’s meeting in Melbourne sadly bun-
gled it. The sum of one shilling was spent in
advertising it, and that went to the Age. Yet
the old hero had a good attendance and a splendid
reception. Mr. J. T. Sitch should never mix his
votes of thanks with announcements of concerts
and prize-fights, no matter for what good purpose
they may be intended.
Sir George Grey’s Sydney Town Hall
speech got exactly a column and a quarter in the
S.M. Herald, and the description of the pro-
ceedings another quarter-col. ; total, col -and-half,
not so much space as Granny usually devotes to a
church bun-chewing contest. In the same issue
Dan. O’Connor got nearly half a column for the
adjectival bosh he slung at Sara Bernhardt, and
Single-tax Foxall was allowed two columns of self-
advertising space.

sponding to the toast of the health of the Victorian
Rifle team in London, declared that “ no popula-

As Parkes is “going to live to 120,” let
us put this on record : —
Gentlemen opposite said that in the Commonwealth
Protection would be certain. If that were so he (Si 1 '
Henry Parkes) would be banished from public life to the
end of his days, because he could never take part in a
Protectionist Government. He did not pretend to foresee
events, but he knew this from the best of the light with
which he was endowed by his Maker, that no law or policy
which hindered the freedom of individual life could be
sound or in accordance with the Divine teaching of Jesus
Christ. (Cheers.)
Parkes here not only swears never-failing antago-
nism to Protection, but insinuates that he will
not sit in Parliament, except as a holder of place
and pay.
The Bulletin predicts that the Queens-
land “ conspirators ” will not serve their three
years’sentences. Judge Harding, in his zeal for
“law and order,” has sadly overdone it. Had he
given each man six weeks’ gaol without hard
labour, and threatened bigger penalties next time,
he would have taken the diplomatic course and
the one most conducive to the welfare of Vested
Interests. The three years’ sentences simply
mean a battle-cry for associated labour. Judge
Harding has been rubbing nitric acid into the
cancer—equally by his absurd sentences and the
method in which he conducted the trial. He has
proved himself unfit to be a judge and should at
once be removed from the bench.
The kernel of the recommendations of the
N.S.W. Labour Commission is the establishment
by statute law of a Board of Conciliation and
Arbitration. In the conciliation branch of ad-
ministration, the recognition of the tribunal by
the parties to the dispute is to be purely optional;
in the arbitration branch it is to be gwcm-cornpul-
sory. Conciliation is to precede arbitration where-
ever possible. The Board is to consist of permanent
and removeable members. The permanent mem-
bers are to be the Arbitration Board proper ; the
whole.of the members —viz., the permanent ones
and others chosen by the parties disputing—are to
be the Conciliation Board. The permanent mem-
bers are to be not less than three in number —the
chairman to be appointed by the Government and
to be a neutral man ; the remainder to be chosen
equally by employed and employers, or committees
chosen by those parties to select their respective
judges.
